Jackson v. Commonwealth


No. 388 M.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


143 A.3d 468; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 301


June 6, 2016, Argued 
July 7, 2016, Decided 
July 7, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because Pennsylvania sexual offenders who completed their 10-year registration period before the Sexual Offender Registration and Notification Act (SORNA) was enacted were not required to register for the rest of their lives, requiring petitioner, whose offense was committed in Texas, to register for the rest of his life under SORNA and not count all the years that he had registered in other states was not reasonably related to enhancing public safety and welfare, and thus violated his rights under the Equal Protection Clause of the U.S. Constitution.


OUTCOME: The appellate court granted petitioner's application for summary relief.

 

1602 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Czarnecki v. State Emples. Ret. Bd.


No. 1225 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


143 A.3d 460; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 305


May 11, 2016, Argued 
July 6, 2016, Decided 
July 6, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2007-08. State Employees' Retirement Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the on-call service an employee provided to the Department of Public Welfare should have been included in the final average salary component of State Employees' Retirement Board's calculation to determine his retirement benefit. HOLDINGS: [1]-The Board's exclusion of on-call pay from retirement-covered compensation was clearly erroneous and unsupported by the State Employees' Retirement Code because on-call pay was "compensation" under the Retirement Code, 71 Pa.C.S. § 5102, since it was not expressly excluded from the statutory definition; [2]-On-call pay was set the collective bargaining agreement, and payments for on-call service were made on a regular basis, as contractually owed; [3]-The employee performed "actual work" while on-call, and the Retirement Code did not require compensation to correspond to actual work performed.


OUTCOME: Order reversed.

 

1603 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Costa v. Cortes


No. 251 M.D. 2016


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


142 A.3d 1004; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 307


June 9, 2016, Argued 
July 6, 2016, Decided 
July 6, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by, Motion dismissed by, As moot Costa v. Cortes, 145 A.3d 721, 2016 Pa. LEXIS 1959 (Pa., Sept. 2, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Costa v. Cortes, 2016 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 292 (2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The respondent senators were entitled to summary relief under Pa.R.A.P. 1532(b) and the petitioner senators were not entitled to mandamus relief because a concurrent resolution regarding a proposed constitutional amendment did not violate the advertising requirements of Pa. Const. art. XI, § 1, the resolution was a valid exercise of the General Assembly's exclusive power under § 1, the resolution echoed existing law with respect to the Secretary's duties under 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2621(f), the resolution was not a legislative bill and did not violate the single-subject requirements of Pa. Const. art. III, § 3, the resolution did not disenfranchise voters, and the proposed amendment could be placed before the electorate during the general election without violating § 1.


OUTCOME: deny Petitioners' application for summary relief, grant Respondents' application for summary relief, and enter judgment in favor of Respondents.

RB Alden Corp. v. Commonwealth


No. 73 F.R. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


142 A.3d 169; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 278


May 12, 2016, Argued 
June 15, 2016, Decided 
June 15, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Objection overruled by, Dismissed by, As moot, in part RB Alden Corp. v. Commonwealth, 2017 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 704 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Sept. 12, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 1000719. Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court reversed the order classifying the sale of a partnership interest as business income and imposing an assessed corporate net income tax (CNIT) because the net loss carryover capping provision contained in 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7401(3)(4)(c)(1)(A)(II) was unconstitutional in violation of the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, as it prevented the taxpayers from reducing their taxable income to $ 0 and cause them to pay CNIT by capping the deduction they could take.


OUTCOME: Order reversed; case remanded to Department was directed to calculate the corporate net income tax without capping the amount that it can take on its net loss carryover.

Lil Shining Stars, Inc. v. Dep't of Human Servs.


No. 693 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

January 22, 2016, Submitted 
June 8, 2016, Decided 
June 8, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 018-14-0005. State Agency: Department of Human Services.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-62 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1016 and its accompanying regulations did not infringe on the right of petitioner, which operated a child care center, to be free from an unreasonable search under the Fourth Amendment, as petitioner was engaged in a commercial activity that was heavily regulated and the applicable provisions were clear that petitioner was subject to inspections, both announced and unannounced; [2]-Petitioner had not shown the substantial compliance required for a provisional license under 62 Pa. Doe v. Franklin County


No. 1634 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


139 A.3d 296; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 225


March 7, 2016, Argued 
May 20, 2016, Decided 
May 20, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Doe v. Franklin Cnty., 161 A.3d 800, 2016 Pa. LEXIS 2861 (Pa., Dec. 21, 2016)
Reversed by, in part, Remanded by Doe v. Franklin Cty., 2017 Pa. LEXIS 3178 (Pa., Nov. 22, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 4623-2014. Common Pleas Court of the County of Franklin. Kurtz, Senior Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred in sustaining preliminary objections to an action by firearm licensees, challenging how the county sheriff's office and related others managed issuing, renewing, revoking, and denying licenses to carry firearms, as their allegations that sending the information on an unenveloped postcard violated the confidentiality requirement of 18 Pa.C.S. § 6111(i) of the Pennsylvania Uniform Firearms Act of 1995, 18 Pa.C.S. §§ 6101-6127, stated a potentially actionable claim; [2]-Alleged violations of 18 Pa.C.S. § 6109(h) by the sheriff's failure to either send the licensees' renewal notices or refund a portion of the prior application fee that included a renewal notice lacked merit, as there was no private statutory remedy.


OUTCOME: Order reversed in part; affirmed in part. Matter remanded for further proceedings.

Dougherty v. Pa. State Police


No. 537 M.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


138 A.3d 152; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 196


September 16, 2015, Argued 
April 27, 2016, Decided 
April 27, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Where petitioner filed suit to compel the Pennsylvania State Police (PSP) to change his registration status under the Sexual Offender Registration and Notification Act (SORNA), the court sustained PSP's preliminary objection (POs) to his claim that the retroactive increase in his registration period violated the Ex Post Facto Clause; [2]-It overruled PSP's PO regarding internet notification provisions added to SORNA since his conviction, as it was not sure they were not punitive; [2]-It sustained PSP's PO to petitioner's claim that the increase in his registration period violated his due process rights under the U.S. and Pennsylvania Constitutions, as he did not identify any interest protected by due process; [3]-As PSP was not a party to petitioner's plea agreement, the court sustained its PO alleging that it could not be liable for a breach of that agreement.


OUTCOME: The POs were sustained in part and overruled in part.

Costa v. Cortes


No. 251 M.D. 2016


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


143 A.3d 430; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 310


April 19, 2016, Heard 
April 20, 2016, Decided 
April 20, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Publication Ordered July 7, 2016.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Costa v. Cortes, 2016 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 292 (2016)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Senators' application to preliminarily enjoin the Commonwealth Secretary from implementing a resolution which purported to, inter alia, remove a proposed constitutional amendment from the primary election ballot and place it on the later general election ballot lacked merit, as Pa. Const. art. XI, § 1 vested the exclusive authority to determine time, manner, and process for constitutional amendments in the General Assembly; [2]-Moreover, the senators did not demonstrate a substantial legal question with respect to the alleged infringement on qualified electors' rights or a violation of Pa. Const. art. III, § 9, as the General Assembly had the power and authority to effect the change; [3]-The General Assembly's decision did not conflict with various provisions of existing statutory and constitutional duties, such that injunctive relief was not warranted on that issue.


OUTCOME: Request for preliminary injunction denied.

Commonwealth v. $34,440.00 United States Currency


No. 1021 C.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


138 A.3d 102; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 173


December 9, 2015, Submitted 
April 19, 2016, Decided 
April 19, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 11208 CIVIL 2009. Common Pleas Court of the County of Monroe. Harlacher Sibum, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The court affirmed the order forfeiting $ 34,440 to the Commonwealth because it presented credited evidence to establish that the $ 34,440 was in close proximity to the controlled substances in the vehicle thereby triggering the rebuttable presumption set forth in the Forfeiture Act, 42 Pa.C.S. § 6801(a)(6)(ii), and the claimant did not rebut that presumption with credited evidence; [2]-The court held that, in general, Pennsylvania Courts have held that cash found within arm's length of controlled substances is in sufficiently close proximity to trigger the rebuttable presumption in favor of forfeiture.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

Germantown Cab Co. v. Phila. Parking Auth.


No. 1078 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


134 A.3d 1115; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 102


February 9, 2016, Argued 
March 1, 2016, Decided 
March 1, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 2, 2016.
Rehearing denied by Germantown Cab Co. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 176 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Apr. 20, 2016)
Appeal denied by Germantown Cab Co. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 2016 Pa. LEXIS 2481 (Pa., Nov. 1, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. October Term 2014, No. 02359. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Wright Padilla, J.
Germantown Cab Co. v. Bureau of Admin. Adjudication, 2015 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 209 (2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Philadelphia, Pennsylvania, Parking Authority acted in accordance with its power to impound a certified motor carrier's taxicab because the Parking Authority Law, 53 Pa.C.S. § 5714, authorized the impoundment of vehicles for violating the Authority's regulations, and the carrier violated 52 Pa. Code § 1017.32 by failing to have a current Taxicab and Limousine Division (TLD) inspection sticker before providing service in Philadelphia; [2]-The carrier did not meet its burden in proving that § 5714(a) and (g) and the Authority's regulations clearly, palpably, and plainly violated the Constitution; [3]-The Authority acted in accordance with the power bestowed upon it by the Legislature, and it impounded the carrier's taxicab because it was not TLD inspected and thus was a safety concern.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

 

1611 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Blake v. State Civ. Serv. Comm'n


No. 724 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


133 A.3d 812; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 81


October 9, 2015, Submitted 
February 17, 2016, Decided 
February 17, 2016, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Blake v. State Civil Serv. Comm'n, 158 A.3d 1245, 2016 Pa. LEXIS 2297 (Pa., Oct. 13, 2016)
Reversed by Blake v. State Civ. Serv. Comm'n, 2017 Pa. LEXIS 1739 (Pa., July 25, 2017)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The State Civil Service Commission erred in denying a veteran a preference in his application for civil service employment because the veteran, who was honorably discharged by the United States Army, engaged in service that supported his claim that he was a soldier for the purpose of applying a veteran's preference under the Military and Veterans Code, 51 Pa.C.S. § 7102; [2]-Pursuant to the Code, 51 Pa.C.S. § 7101, the veteran was a soldier when he attended West Point, and because he was a cadet, he was on active duty for the purposes of 38 U.S.C.S. § 101(21)(D); [3]-The veteran was on active duty between July 1991 and January 1993, a period recognized for veteran's preference purposes as satisfying the requirement to be a "veteran" under 5 U.S.C.S. § 2108(1); [4]-The term "active duty" for purposes of the Code was consistent with the definition in § 101(21).


OUTCOME: Order reversed.

Taylor v. Pa. State Police of Pa.


No. 532 M.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


132 A.3d 590; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 49


September 16, 2015, Argued 
January 12, 2016, Decided 
January 12, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether to grant the Pennsylvania State Police's (PSP's) preliminary objections (POs) to a petition challenging the constitutionality of the Sexual Offender Registration and Notification Act's (SORNA's) registration and notification requirements. HOLDINGS: [1]-The PSP's PO to petitioner's ex post facto challenge to SORNA's internet notification provision, 42 Pa.C.S. § 9799.28, was overruled as the court could not say with certainty that Pa. Const. art. I, § 17's Ex Post Facto Clause did not provide more protection than its federal counterpart in this regard; [2]-The court overruled the PSP's PO to petitioner's claim that SORNA's irrebuttable presumption that all sexual offenders posed a high risk of reoffending violated his due process rights, as he was entitled to present evidence regarding sexual offenders' recidivism rates in an attempt to show the presumption was unfounded.


OUTCOME: The POs were sustained in part and overruled in part.

Konyk v. Pa. State Police of Pa.


No. 538 M.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


133 A.3d 96; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 48


September 16, 2015, Argued 
January 12, 2016, Decided 
January 12, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Petitioner did not allege the facts necessary to survive the demurrer the Pennsylvania State Police filed to his petition seeking to compel it to change his sexual offender registration status; [2]-Petitioner failed to allege any facts that could show the Commonwealth was in an implied contractual relationship with him to incorporate the Sexual Offender Registration and Notification Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9799.10-9799.41, as it existed at the time of his plea agreement with the United States.; [3]-Petitioner did not plead that the Commonwealth was involved or participated in the plea negotiations, was an intended beneficiary of the plea agreement, or benefited from the agreement; [4]-His request that the commonwealth court specifically enforce his plea agreement was based upon legal allegations that were unsupported by necessary factual averments.


OUTCOME: Petition for review dismissed with prejudice.

Protz v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd.


No. 402 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


131 A.3d 572; 2016 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 18


December 10, 2015, Argued 
January 6, 2016, Decided 
January 6, 2016, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. A13-0748. State Agency: Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.
Protz v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Derry Area Sch. Dist.), 124 A.3d 406, 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 404 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Sept. 18, 2015)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-When petitioner claimant obtained a medical malpractice award for her medical treatment for a workplace injury, and respondents employer and insurer sought to subrogate that recovery under 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 671, a workers' compensation judge did not err in relying on a physician's report she submitted stating she was injured by a negligently performed knee replacement necessitated by her work injury because the employer offered it as to the subrogation issue, and she did not object on relevancy grounds; [2]-It was not error to award the employer and insurer subrogation as to the claimant's future medical expenses and wage loss because, while 40 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1303.508(a) and (c) barred subrogation as to past expenses and wages, they were silent as to future expenses and wages, so pre-existing law allowing subrogation applied.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

Peake v. Commonwealth


No. 216 M.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


132 A.3d 506; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 585


September 16, 2015, Argued 
December 30, 2015, Decided 
December 30, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-There was merit to a constitutional challenge to the lifetime employment ban of 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10225.503(a) in the Older Adults Protective Services Act, 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 10225.101-10225.5102, which prohibited persons with even one conviction of a list of enumerated crimes to be employed in the care of older adults, because the ban was unconstitutional on its face, such that summary relief was warranted; [2]-The ban violated due process under Pa. Const. art. I, § 1 because it went beyond the necessities of the case and was not substantially related to the Act's stated objective of protecting older adults.


OUTCOME: Summary relief granted.

V.S. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare


No. 1186 C.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


131 A.3d 523; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 586


December 26, 2014, Submitted 
December 30, 2015, Decided


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of 021-12-0528.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Department of Public Welfare properly upheld an administrative denial of a mother's request to expunge her indicated report under 23 Pa.C.S. § 6303(a) from the ChildLine & Abuse Registry, as her request for an appeal was untimely under 23 Pa.C.S. § 6341(a)(2) and a nunc pro tunc exception was not warranted because she did not make the necessary showing or act with reasonable diligence; [2]-The mother's due process rights were not violated because under Mathews, as she received notice, had ample opportunity to be heard, and there was no right to counsel; [3]-The minority tolling provision of 42 Pa.C.S. § 5533(b)(1)(i) was not applicable to the matter, as it did not apply to an appeal from an administrative determination.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

Scarnati v. Wolf


No. 579 M.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


135 A.3d 200; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 580


June 17, 2015, Argued 
December 30, 2015, Decided 
December 30, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by, in part Scarnati v. Wolf, 2017 Pa. LEXIS 3177 (Pa., Nov. 22, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-In an underlying petition for review, challenging the constitutionality of the former governor's (FG's) partial disapproval of appropriations bills, senators established their standing by alleging a sufficient legislative interest as to whether the Governor satisfied constitutional requirements; [2]-Although a temporary, mid-session adjournment of the General Assembly could prevent the return of a bill with objections to the house where the bill originated pursuant to Pa. Pennsylvanians for Union Reform v. Pa. Office of Admin.

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

November 14, 2014, Submitted 
December 18, 2015, Decided 
December 18, 2015, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. AP 2014-0795. State Agency: Office of Open Records.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-An agency properly complied with 65 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 67.901 of the Right to Know Law, 65 P.S. §§ 67.101-67.3104, as it timely substantively determined that records of only two specifically-named Commonwealth employees' political action committee (PAC) contributions were not accessible public records, and it concluded that revealing whether it had such records would disclose otherwise protected information; [2]-The Office of Open Records properly concluded that public disclosure of an employee's PAC contribution deductions would violate his right to freedom of association under U.S. Const. amend. 1621 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Nextel Communs. of the Mid-Atlantic, Inc. v. Commonwealth


No. 98 F.R. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


129 A.3d 1; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 520


September 16, 2015, Argued 
November 23, 2015, Decided 
November 23, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed in part and reversed in part by Nextel Communs. of the Mid-Atlantic, Inc. v. Commonwealth, 2017 Pa. LEXIS 2456 (Pa., Oct. 18, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 1107916. Board of Finance and Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the net loss carryover (NLC) deduction under 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7401(3)(e)(4)(c)(2), in operation or effect for a particular tax year, which was part of the method or formula for computing the corporate net income tax, as applied to the taxpayer, violated the Uniformity Clause in Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. HOLDINGS: [1]-The taxpayer demonstrated that the NLC deduction provision in the Pennsylvania Tax Reform Code of 1971 created classes of taxpayers according to their taxable income. Specifically, the NLC deduction provision for the tax year treated taxpayers with taxable income in excess of $3 million differently than taxpayers with $3 million or less in taxable income; [2]-Because the NLC deduction provision was unconstitutional as applied to the taxpayer for the tax year, the taxpayer was entitled to a refund of corporate net income tax paid in the tax year.


OUTCOME: Order reversed, and tax refund petition granted.

 

1622 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Sandusky v. Pa. State Employees' Ret. Bd.


No. 60 C.D. 2015


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


127 A.3d 34; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 498


October 7, 2015, Argued 
November 13, 2015, Decided 
November 13, 2015, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2012-38. State Agency: Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board.
Commonwealth v. Sandusky, 63 A.3d 253, 2012 Pa. Super. LEXIS 1042 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-It was error for the Pennsylvania State Employees' Retirement Board to affirm that a retiree was a state university "employee," which resulted in forfeiture of his pension under the Public Employee Pension Forfeiture Act, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1311-1315, as he was not an employee and was not on the payroll when his criminal convictions or the underlying criminal acts were committed; [2]-Severance payments were not compensation under 24 Pa.C.S. § 8102, as they were not "regular" payments for services rendered, and the retiree's rehiring was for a limited time on an emergent basis; [3]-The retiree did not qualify as a "school employee" because he did not maintain an employee-employer relationship with the university after his retirement, and his work relating to the university did not fit within that definition where the work was not performed "for" the university.


OUTCOME: Order of Board reversed; pension benefits reinstated. Matter remanded to Board for determination of legal rate of interest.

 

1623 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


McCloskey v. Pa. PUC


No. 1012 C.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


127 A.3d 860; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 478


June 18, 2015, Argued 
November 3, 2015, Decided 
November 3, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by McCloskey v. Pa. PUC, 2015 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 793 (2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. P-2012-2338282, et al. State Agency: Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUES: Whether the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission erred in not requiring a natural gas distribution utility to include an accumulated deferred income tax (ADIT) adjustment in its Distribution System Improvement Charge (DSIC) calculation and whether the utility was permitted to include the State income tax gross-up in its DSIC calculation. HOLDINGS: [1]-The Commission properly approved the utility's DSIC because 66 Pa.C.S. § 1301 only required the DSIC surcharge mechanism as a whole be just and reasonable; [2]-Even without the inclusion of specific additional base rate adjustments in the ADIT calculation, the utility's DSIC surcharge mechanism, which was an alternative ratemaking mechanism, was consistent with the implementation of 2012 Pa. Laws 72; [3]-With the imposition of an earnings rate cap, as a whole, the overall effect of the calculated DSIC rate was just and reasonable.


OUTCOME: Commission's decision affirmed.

Parker Ave., L.P. v. City of Philadelphia

May 6, 2015, Argued 
July 30, 2015, Decided 
July 30, 2015, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. May Term, 2013, No. 1590. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Ceisler, J.
Parker Ave., L.P. v. City of Philadelphia, 2014 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 302 (2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The trial court erred in concluding that a city's failure to enact street paving ordinances was a de facto taking of an LP's property, as the LP was aware before the property's purchase that it needed the city council's support to proceed with a proposed development; thus, nothing was "taken" from the LP that it had or legitimately expected to get; [2]-The trial court erred in concluding that developing a residential subdivision was the highest and best use of the property, as the evidence established it was remote and speculative; the LP had not met its burden of proof showing that the alleged detriment underlying the claim of de facto taking was the direct and necessary consequence of the city's failure to pass the paving ordinances.

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a borough properly used Borough of Gettysburg, Pa., Property Maintenance Code Ordinance § 705.1 to require a property owner to make structural changes to its building or lose the use of basement apartments. CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-A facial state constitutional challenge to Pa. I, § 10, or 18 Pa.C.S. § 109(1) because the citation implicated a different ordinance, different elements, and different facts than in prior proceedings; [3]-The citation was sufficiently specific pursuant to Pa. P. 403(A)(6) because it alleged the basic elements of the charged offense under Walnutport, Pa., Municipal Solid Waste Ordinance 2011-05, § 16, such that dismissal was not warranted; [4]-The violation was supported by proof beyond a reasonable doubt of defendant's obligation, and failure, to pay the fee.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

Derry St. Pub, Inc. v. Pa. State Police


No. 331 C.D. 2014


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


111 A.3d 1240; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 117


September 11, 2014, Argued 
March 24, 2015, Decided 
March 24, 2015, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Derry St. Pub, Inc. v. Pa. State Police, Bureau of Liquor Control Enforcement, 633 Pa. 770, 125 A.3d 1203, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 2152 (Sept. 29, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. 2013-CV-6753-MP. Common Pleas Court of the County of Dauphin. Curcillo, J.
Derry St. Pub v. Pa. State Police, 2014 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 355 (Feb. 5, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the renewal of a liquor license properly included the restrictions of a conditional liquor agreement (CLA). HOLDINGS: [1]-A citation for the liquor licensee not adhering to the terms of the CLA was valid; [2]-The CLA was not terminated or otherwise repudiated by the Pennsylvania Liquor Control Board; [3]-The CLA scheme was not an unconstitutional delegation of legislative power, as the CLA was a voluntary mechanism for the licensee to otherwise preserve its license after citation and revocation; [4]-The licensee voluntarily entered into the CLA to maintain its liquor license that would otherwise have been revoked due to violations of the Pennsylvania Liquor Code, 47 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 1-101 -- 10-1001; [5]-The licensee was bound to the CLA's restrictions; [6]-The licensee's liquor license was accordingly renewed under the burden of the CLA's restrictions.


OUTCOME: Judgment affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Bethel Township, Pa., Local Ordinance 1197-4 § 3.C provided a reasonable standard by which a person could gauge his conduct because it used common everyday language easily understood by a person of ordinary intelligence, which defined and prohibited conduct that was highly contextual and applied only to specifically defined behavior, only when directed at police officers, and only when those officers were exercising their official duties; [2]-Appellant's conduct fell squarely within the terms of the Ordinance's prohibition against threatening to molest a police officer in the exercise of her duties because he threatened the police officer with the buckshot loaded in his shotgun; [3]-Appellant knew he was speaking to a police officer and, with that knowledge, made a direct threat of serious harm to that police officer.

110 A.3d 1103; 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 84


December 10, 2014, Argued 
February 27, 2015, Decided 
February 27, 2015, Filed


NOTICE: An unreported opinion of the Commonwealth Court may be cited and relied upon when it is relevant under the doctrine of law of the case, res judicata or collateral estoppel. Parties may also cite an unreported panel decision of the Commonwealth Court issued after January 15, 2008 for its persuasive value, but not as binding precedent. A single-judge opinion of the Commonwealth Court, even if reported, shall be cited only for its persuasive value, not as a binding precedent.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reconsideration denied by, Reconsideration denied by, En banc In re Fry, 2015 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 144 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Apr. 6, 2015)

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-With respect to a constable's petition for approval of appointment of a deputy constable pursuant to 44 Pa.C.S. § 7122, the constable was not required to demonstrate the requisite necessity because the court overruled the needs test crafted in In re Hunter, 782 A.2d 610 (Pa. Cmwlth. 2001), upon finding that there was no sound policy reason for such a showing; [2]-The District Attorney had standing in the petition pursuant to § 7122 because he had a sufficient interest in the court's discretionary appointment of a deputy constable; [3]-Affirmance of the denial of the constable's petition was warranted because as the deputy constable no longer resided in the borough, he was not eligible for the appointment under § 7122.


OUTCOME: Order affirmed.

Commonwealth v. 1997 Chevrolet

106 A.3d 836; 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 577


May 14, 2014, Argued 
December 17, 2014, Decided 
December 17, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by Commonwealth v. 1997 Chevrolet & Contents Seized from James Young, 632 Pa. 440, 120 A.3d 993, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 1675 (July 30, 2015)

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Appealed from No. CP-51-MD-0013471-2010 and CP-51-MD-0002972-2010. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Patrick, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the trial court erred in granting the Commonwealth's petition for the forfeiture of appellant's home and vehicle under the Controlled Substances Forfeiture Act based on her son's drug sales. HOLDINGS: [1]-The court erred in applying the United States v. Bajakajian test for determining whether the forfeiture was grossly disproportional to the gravity of the offense, in violation of U.S. Const. amend. OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

Pa. Fed'n of Dog Clubs v. Commonwealth


No. 421 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


105 A.3d 51; 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 544


August 15, 2014, Submitted 
November 19, 2014, Decided 
November 19, 2014, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed without opinion by Pa. Fed'n of Dog Clubs v. Commonwealth, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 1124 (Pa., May 26, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Petitioner humane societies were aggrieved and had standing to maintain an action against the Commonwealth because they satisfied all three prongs of the standing test enumerated in Johnson; [2]-Laches did not apply because the Commonwealth had not presented evidence demonstrating prejudice from a lapse of time; [3]-Petitioners' complaint could not be dismissed based on sovereign immunity because sovereign immunity did not bar a declaratory judgment action or injunction seeking to prohibit state parties from acting; [4]-Because petitioners did not retain a vested entitlement under the Due Process Clause to have the money in the Dog Law Restricted Account (DLRA) utilized in the manner directed by statute, they had no vested right in the DLRA funds and the court could not declare that extinguishing petitioners' vested rights was an illegal taking of their members' property.


OUTCOME: The Commonwealth's application for summary relief under Pa.R.A.P. 1532(b) was granted, petitioners' amended complaint was dismissed with prejudice, and judgment was entered in the Commonwealth's favor.

Spencer v. City of Reading Charter Bd.


No. 1377 C.D. 2013


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

May 13, 2014, Argued 
August 8, 2014, Decided 
August 8, 2014, Filed

Spencer v. City of Reading Charter Bd., 97 A.3d 844, 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 402 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Aug. 8, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a court of common pleas properly reversed the Reading, Pennsylvania Charter Board's adjudication charging the mayor with violations of Reading, Pennsylvania's Charter. HOLDINGS: [1]-The Charter Board had standing to appeal because the Charter Board had a direct and substantial interest in the appeal in that the trial court's ruling directly conflicted with the Charter Board's ability to enforce and interpret the Charter; [2]-The trial court did not err in applying the capricious disregard standard in finding that the Charter Board provided no explanation for its rejection of a declaration by the city's managing director, which was highly relevant and competent evidence as the managing director was the person who directly hired the subject employees. OUTCOME: Motion to quash denied. Order of the trial court affirmed.

Spencer v. City of Reading Charter Bd.

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

August 8, 2014, Decided 
August 8, 2014, Filed

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether a court of common pleas properly set aside the City of Reading, Pa., Charter Board's imposition of sanctions upon the mayor of Reading, Pennsylvania for performing the duties of the city's managing director at a time that the position was vacant. HOLDINGS: [1]-The Charter Board had standing to appeal because it had a direct and substantial interest in the appeal as the trial court's ruling directly conflicted with the Charter Board's ability to enforce and interpret the Charter; [2]-The trial court did not err in holding that Reading, Pa., Charter Board Advisory Opinion No. 22 provided a valid defense to the charges against the mayor and in finding that the mayor properly relied upon the Advisory Opinion, which stated that the mayor could not exercise his appointment power until 90 days had passed following the first day of vacancy in the managing director position.


OUTCOME: Motion to quash appeal denied. Order of the trial court affirmed.

1668 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Ctr. Twp.


No. 522 M.D. 2013


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


95 A.3d 354; 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 331


April 23, 2014, Argued 
June 24, 2014, Decided 
June 24, 2014, Filed


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the Pennsylvania Office of Open Records (OOR) or respondent township was entitled to summary judgment in the OOR's action seeking an order compelling the township to produce requested records in unredacted form for in camera inspection. HOLDINGS: [1]-The OOR had subject matter jurisdiction under Pennsylvania's Right-to-Know Law (RTKL) to determine if a document was exempt under the attorney-client privilege, work-product doctrine, or ethics-based rule of confidentiality, as its involvement in the RTKL process did not infringe on the state supreme court's exclusive authority under Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c) to govern the conduct of attorneys; [2]-The OOR had the implied authority under the RTKL to conduct in camera review of the documents to ascertain whether the township's redactions were proper under the attorney-client privilege or the attorney work-product doctrine.


OUTCOME: The OOR's motion for summary judgment was granted.

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-Because it was charged with determining the public nature of records requested under the Right-to-Know Law (RTKL), the Office of Open Records (OOR) had the authority to interpret federal statutes that involved public records and public access to agency information, including the Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act (EPCRA); [2]-Because the EPCRA did not state that Tier II hazardous chemical information "shall be public" or the like, the OOR erred in concluding that EPCRA established the public nature of Tier II information; [3]-Because 42 U.S.C.S. § 11022(e)(3) required that the requester identify a specific facility, the RTKL provisions for greater access would not apply pursuant to 65 Pa. In re Appeal of Silverman

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

February 12, 2014, Argued 
April 25, 2014, Decided 
April 25, 2014, Filed

Appealed from No. CP 51 CR 0105361-2003. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Dembe, President Judge.
Commonwealth v. Mayo, 956 A.2d 298, 2008 Pa. Super. LEXIS 5316 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the president judge (PJ) erred in awarding reduced fees to counsel appointed to represent defendant convicted of murder in Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA) proceedings. HOLDINGS: [1]-Counsel had a right to appeal the fee award, which was final because it was one of two orders that resolved all the PCRA litigation; [2]-Joinder of the city was not mandatory; [3]-In the absence of government compulsion to represent defendant, there was no prohibited "taking" from counsel; [3]-Counsel was afforded all the process due under the local rules; having voluntarily submitted to that process, he could not later object to it; [4]-The PJ was not required to delineate each reason for disallowance of every aspect of the fee request; she explained her decision in a manner sufficient for appellate review; [5]-There was no abuse of discretion or error of law in the entry of the award.


OUTCOME: The fee award was affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:

CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: HOLDINGS: [1]-The Unemployment Compensation Board of Review erred in determining that the claimant was ineligible for benefits under 43 Pa. Stat. OUTCOME: Order reversed.

PRIOR HISTORY: Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


OVERVIEW: ISSUE: Whether the Pennsylvania Department of Health (DOH) was entitled to a writ of mandamus compelling the clerk of the orphans' court to perform his statutory duties under the marriage laws and refrain from issuing marriage licenses to same-sex couples. HOLDINGS: [1]-As the clerk was an officer of state government under 42 Pa.C.S. §102, the appellate court had original jurisdiction over the action under 42 Pa.C.S. § 761(a)(1); [2]-DOH had standing to bring the action based on its statutory duty to enforce state marriage law, including 23 Pa.C.S. §§ 1102 and 1704, which banned same-sex marriages in Pennsylvania; [3]-The clerk had no authority to interpret statutes he was charged with enforcing; [4]-As only courts could determine the constitutionality of statutes, the clerk lacked standing to assert the purported unconstitutionality of statutes as a defense to the mandamus petition.

Pa. State Ass'n of Jury Comm'rs v. Commonwealth

1693 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Pentlong Corp. v. GLS Capital, Inc.


No. 891 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


72 A.3d 818; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 264


April 16, 2013, Argued 
July 15, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. GD98-005800. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Wettick, J.
Pentlong Corp. v. GLS Capital, Inc., 573 Pa. 34, 820 A.2d 1240, 2003 Pa. LEXIS 383 (2003)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand in a dispute regarding defendant assignee of a county's delinquent taxes' ability to collect attorney's and other fees, the Common Pleas Court of Allegheny County, Pennsylvania, granted plaintiff class of delinquent taxpayers' (class) summary judgment motion and dismissed the class's claims challenging the assignee's ability to collect attorneys' fees, assignment fees and revival fees. The class appealed.


OVERVIEW: The appellate court noted that the Pennsylvania Municipal Claims and Tax Liens Act (MCTLA), 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7101 et seq., had been amended to authorize municipalities to collect reasonable attorneys' fees from delinquent taxpayers retroactive to January 1, 1996 in response to a holding in this case that the assignee could not collect attorneys' fees from delinquent taxpayers. The state's highest court held that the amendment was constitutional. The appellate court held that Pentlong II was binding as to the named class members. The class had not been certified when the MCTLA was amended. Thus, the assignee could collect attorneys' fees from the non-named class members. An assignment of delinquent taxes was not an act in lieu of collecting taxes. The fee to effect such an assignment to a third party was a prothonotary fee incurred to collect the taxes and could be collected by the assignee. There was no necessity element in evaluating what fees could be collected by the county or its assignee. The assignee could also collect revival fees. The revivals that occurred as part of the process to effect the assignment of the liens en masse to the assignee were proper.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed with respect to the named class members, and with respect to those members, the matter was remanded to the trial court to fashion appropriate relief. In all other respects, the judgment was affirmed.

 

1694 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Funk v. Commonwealth


No. 713 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


71 A.3d 1097; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 235


May 16, 2013, Argued 
July 3, 2013, Decided 
July 3, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner applicant filed an action for injunctive and declaratory relief to require the Pennsylvania Department of Environmental Protection (DEP) to submit her petition for rule-making with respect to fossil fuel carbon dioxide emissions to the Pennsylvania Environmental Quality Board (EQB). The DEP filed preliminary objections, asserting that the petition for review was barred by the doctrine of exhaustion of administrative remedies.


OVERVIEW: The DEP contended that the applicant's petition for review was barred by her failure to exhaust administrative remedies. The applicant did not dispute that she was able to challenge the DEP's refusal to submit her petition for rule-making to the EQB by appeal to the Pennsylvania Environmental Hearing Board (EHB) and that the EHB was able to grant the relief which she sought. Indeed, she had filed an appeal with the EHB. Her justification for not exhausting her remedies before the EHB prior to seeking relief in the court was that she had asserted constitutional challenges to the DEP's decision. The court found that, because the applicant's constitutional arguments were challenges to the DEP's application of the statutes to her case, not facial challenges to the statutes as a whole, they were not exempt from the requirement that she was to exhaust her remedy of raising these issues in her EHB appeal before seeking judicial review. Moreover, she did not assert constitutional challenges to all of the grounds on which the DEP based its decision. Therefore, because the applicant did not exhaust her administrative remedy of appeal to the EHB, her petition for review was to be dismissed.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections were sustained, and the petition for review was dismissed.

Zauflik v. Pennsbury Sch. Dist.


No. 1219 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


72 A.3d 773; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 233


February 11, 2013, Argued 
July 3, 2013, Decided 
July 3, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, in part, Appeal denied by, in part Zauflik v. Pennsbury Sch. Dist., 624 Pa. 1, 84 A.3d 293, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 161 (Pa., 2014)
Affirmed by Zauflik v. Pennsbury Sch. Dist., 629 Pa. 1, 104 A.3d 1096, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 3030 (Pa., Nov. 19, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2007-09693. Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks. Mellon, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant student sought review of orders from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks (Pennsylvania), which granted post-trial relief to appellee school district and, inter alia, reduced a jury verdict for the student to reflect application of 42 Pa.C.S. § 8553 of the Political Subdivision Tort Claims Act, and imposed delay damages only on the molded verdict. The matter arose in the student's negligence action.


OVERVIEW: The student was run over by a school bus that ran off the road due to undisputed driver negligence, resulting in catastrophic injuries that included a partial leg amputation. She filed suit against the district and a jury verdict was entered in her favor. By post-trial orders, the trial court molded the jury verdict, which was over $14 million, to reflect the application of § 8553. Further, delay damages were added to the molded, rather than original, verdict, and sanctions were imposed against the district under Pa.R.C.P. No. 4019. On appeal, the court found no merit to the student's facial and as applied challenges to the Act. Based on judicial precedent, there was no violation of Pa. Const. art. I, § 11 or Pa. Const. art. III, § 18. Further, there was no violation of the jury trial right under Pa. Const. art. I, § 6, or of the equal protection guarantee under a heightened level of scrutiny. The cap also did not violate the separation of powers. The district's purchase of insurance above the cap amount did not constitute a waiver of the cap. A monetary sanction against the district for its failure to disclose the existence of an excess insurance policy was proper under Rule 4019.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the orders of the trial court.

Washington v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare


No. 602 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


71 A.3d 1070; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 224


April 17, 2013, Argued 
June 24, 2013, Decided 
June 24, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Decision reached on appeal by Washington v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare of Pa., 76 A.3d 536, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2179 (Pa., 2013)
Petition denied by Washington v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare of Pa., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 3237 (Pa., Dec. 24, 2013)
Reconsideration denied by Wash. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare of Pa., 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 101 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Feb. 7, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed from No. ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, individuals and associations, filed a petition which challenged an Act, 2012 Pa. Laws 668, No. 80, which amended the Pennsylvania Public Welfare Code, 62 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 101 - 1503. The individuals and associations sought to enjoin enforcement of the Act. Respondent, the Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare, filed preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer to all six counts of the petition for review.


OVERVIEW: Amendments to the Pennsylvania Public Welfare Code affected a number of human services programs administered by the Pennsylvania Department of Public Welfare. The court found that (1) the Act did not offend Pa. Const. art. III, § 1 because the house bill's original purpose could be understood from the text of the Act to be the regulation and funding of human services programs regulated by the Department of Public Welfare; (2) the Act did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 3 because all the elements of the Act could be unified under the theme of improving the effectiveness and efficiency of the delivery of human services programs to people in need; (3) the Act did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 4 because the Act underwent three readings in each chamber of the Pennsylvania General Assembly; (4) the allegations by the associations established their standing to lodge a constitutional challenge; (5) the application of Pa. Const. art. III, § 24 to the Act was not clear and free from doubt; and (6) certain provisions of the Act appeared to vest legislative authority in the Department of Public Welfare, in violation of the separation of powers under Pa. Const. art. II, § 1.


OUTCOME: The demurrer to the counts which were found not to be unconstitutional was sustained, and those counts were dismissed. The demurrer to the remaining counts was overruled.

Bucks County Servs. v. Phila. Parking Auth.


No. 584 M.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


71 A.3d 379; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 201


February 15, 2013, Submitted 
June 10, 2013, Decided 
June 10, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Summary judgment granted, in part, summary judgment denied, in part by Bucks County Servs. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 104 A.3d 604, 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 540 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Nov. 14, 2014)
Findings of fact/conclusions of law at, Judgment entered by Bucks County Servs. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 2016 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 896 (Nov. 28, 2016)
Amended by Bucks County Servs. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 2017 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 616 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Jan. 3, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Bucks County Servs. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 614 Pa. 547, 38 A.3d 821, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 374 (Feb. 22, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxicab companies (TCs) filed an amended petition for review, seeking declaratory, injunctive, and mandamus relief against respondents, a city parking authority (PA) and the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission (PUC), with respect to their attempt to impose and enforce fines and assessments, and to enforce regulations. The PA and PUC filed preliminary objections (POs)


OVERVIEW: Six of the seven TCs were partial-rights, non-medallion TCs which received certificates of public convenience from the PUC that authorized them to provide service in certain areas of the city. After authority was transferred from the PUC to the PA and regulations were enacted, the PA attempted to enforce the regulations. The court previously agreed with a TC in its petition for review that the regulations were invalid. However, the PA continued to enforce the regulations, and to demand payment of fines and assessments. The TCs filed their petition for review, and respondents filed multiple POs. The court held that the PUC should have been joined as a necessary party to a particular count. The failure to join indispensable parties to a declaratory judgment claim was fatal under 42 Pa.C.S. § 7540(a). As pre-enforcement review of the regulations was warranted, there was no need to exhaust administrative remedies. Further, the claims satisfied the ripeness test, they were not barred by collateral estoppel, and there was no similar prior pending action. A jurisdictional challenge lacked merit. However, there was no basis to seek fees or costs under 42 Pa.C.S. § 2503(9).


OUTCOME: The court sustained POs with respect to the failure to join the PUC to a claim, failure to join indispensable parties, and as to the request for fees and costs. The court dismissed a declaratory judgment and related claims that lacked the indispensable parties, directed the PUC to be joined to the other claim, and dismissed the fees and cost request. The remainder of the POs were overruled.

Schmader v. Cranberry Twp. Bd. of Supervisors


No. 1576 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

April 16, 2013, Argued 
June 7, 2013, Decided 
June 7, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 556-2012. Common Pleas Court of the County of Venango. White, Senior Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Venango County (Pennsylvania), which quashed his appeal as untimely from a decision of appellee, a township zoning hearing board (board).


OVERVIEW: Appellant owned property located in the township where he operated a commercial office for a corporation. The property consisted of two parcels and was zoned residential. Appellant received two zoning enforcement orders stating that he was in violation of the township code because he was operating a business on the property, which was not a permitted use or a use allowed by special exception or conditional use in the residential district. Appellant was ordered to cease operation of his business on the property. Appellant challenged the board's decision by appealing to the trial court. The trial court held that the appeal was untimely since appellant failed to file his appeal within 30 days of receipt of the board's decision. The court found error, noting that the board failed to set forth its date of mailing its decision to appellant. As a result of the board's failure, appellant was justified in filing his appeal within 30 days of receipt of the board's decision.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Scarantino v. Public Sch. Emples. Ret. Bd.


No. 2383 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


68 A.3d 375; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 175


June 4, 2012, Argued 
May 29, 2013, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Release for Publication May 29, 2013.
Appeal denied by Scarantino v. Public Sch. Emples. Ret. Bd., 79 A.3d 1100, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2654 (Pa., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2009-14. State Agency: Public School Employees' Retirement Board.
Scarantino v. Public Sch. Employees' Ret. Bd., 2013 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 271 (2013)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a former public school superintendent, appealed the order of respondent, the Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement Board (Board), which determined that his retirement benefits were forfeited under the Public Employee Pension Forfeiture Act, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1313, as a result of his guilty plea to a federal criminal offense.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner pled guilty to violating 18 U.S.C.S. § 666(a)(1)(B) by knowingly, intentionally, and corruptly accepting $ 5,000 in connection with influencing and rewarding contracts for the school district for which he was employed. Petitioner was discharged for cause and the Board notified him that his right to receive retirement benefits was subject to forfeiture. At issue was whether the federal crime of theft or bribery concerning programs receiving federal funds under § 666(a)(1)(B) was substantially the same as the crime of bribery in official and political matters under 18 Pa.C.S. § 4701 to cause forfeiture of petitioner's pension. The court held that although the third elements of 18 Pa.C.S. § 4701 and 18 U.S.C.S. § 666 were not identical, the elements under each statute were substantially the same. The court rejected petitioner's vagueness and excessive punishment challenges to 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1313, noting that the forfeiture of petitioner's pension resulted from a breach of the contract between petitioner and the Board. Thus, the forfeiture of pension benefits was not a punitive forfeiture requiring the application of the gross disproportionality analysis.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

McNeil v. Commonwealth


No. 651 F.R. 2010, No. 173 F.R. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


67 A.3d 185; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 168


March 13, 2013, Argued 
May 24, 2013, Decided 
May 24, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 0918849. State Agency: Board of Finance and Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner trustees sought review of orders from respondent, the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania), which upheld the imposition of Pennsylvania income tax (PIT) on all of the income of two inter vivos trusts pursuant to 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 7301(s) and 7302(a).


OVERVIEW: Two inter vivos trusts were created by a Pennsylvania resident in 1959, which were to be governed, administered, and construed under the laws of Delaware. Further, the sole administrative trustee was located in Delaware. None of the trusts' assets or interests in 2007 were located in Pennsylvania, and the trusts had no income from Pennsylvania sources. However, all of the trusts' discretionary beneficiaries were residents of Pennsylvania in 2007. In that year, the Department of Revenue assessed PIT and interest on all of the income of the trusts. Reassessment was denied, and that decision was administratively affirmed by the Board pursuant to 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 7301(s) and 7302(a). On further review, the court found that reliance on a Department ruling was improper, as it did not apply to the trusts at issue. The court found that the Commerce Clause of U.S. Const. art. I, § 8, cl. 3 was violated by the imposition of PIT in the circumstances. The trusts lacked the required physical presence in Pennsylvania to establish a substantial nexus, and the fair apportionment and reasonable relationship prongs of the four-prong test were also not satisfied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the orders of the Board.

Emergycare, Inc. v. Millcreek Twp.


No. 2195 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


68 A.3d 1; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 164


April 15, 2013, Argued 
May 23, 2013, Decided 
May 23, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 11939-2010. Common Pleas Court of the County of Erie. Dunlavey, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant township sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Erie (Pennsylvania), which granted the request of appellees, an emergency medical service (EMS) provider and two customers, to permanently enjoin the township from enforcing Millcreek, Pa., Ordinance No. 2010-3. The Ordinance, in part, designated a particular EMS company as the exclusive entity to provide EMS services.


OVERVIEW: The township board of supervisors enacted the Ordinance, which regulated certain aspects of EMS within the township. The Ordinance expressly designated one company for EMS services. The provider had provided EMS services for 25 years and had contracts with township residents that generated approximately $ 1 million in annual revenue. On the effective date of the Ordinance, appellees sought injunctive relief. A preliminary injunction (PI) was initially issued, and thereafter, a permanent injunction was issued that prevented the township from enforcing the Ordinance. On appeal, the court held that the trial court properly determined that the ordinance violated U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Pa. Const. art. I, § 17, as it substantially impaired the provider's contracts. Further, the township was unable to justify the ordinance with a significant and legitimate public purpose where it was primarily an attempt to isolate a revenue stream and eliminate competition. The issuance of the ex parte PI under Pa.R.C.P. No. 1531(a) was warranted to avoid irreparable harm. Moreover, any objection to the PI was waived by the 17-month delay in challenging it.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Armstrong County Mem. Hosp. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare of Pa.


No. 438 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


67 A.3d 160; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 147


April 17, 2013, Argued 
May 20, 2013, Decided 
May 20, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at, Decision reached on appeal by Armstrong County Mem. Hosp. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare, 83 A.3d 317, 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 40 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Jan. 8, 2014)
Summary judgment granted by, Petition dismissed by Armstrong Cty. Mem'l Hosp. & Monongahela Valley Hosp., Inc. v. Dep't of Pub. Welfare, 2017 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 823 (Oct. 16, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner hospitals filed a petition for review against respondent, the Department of Public Welfare of the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania (DPW), which challenged certain aspects of the implementation of 2010 Pa. Laws 336 (Act 49), which amended the Public Welfare Code, 62 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 101-1503. Intervenor hospital association (HA) opposed the petition, and the DPW and the HA filed preliminary objections to the petition.


OVERVIEW: In resolving the preliminary objections to the hospitals' claims, the court found that as the hospitals' disputes were aimed toward action or inaction by the DPW that was regulatory/legislative in nature, the review afforded by 67 Pa.C.S. § 1102(a) was not applicable. There was nothing in a Letter Agreement between the DPW and the HA that could support any claim for a violation of Pa. Const. art. II, § 1, such that the hospitals' challenge on that basis could not survive. There did not appear to be any provision in 62 P.S. § 443.1(1.2) that could reasonably be construed as nullifying, ex post facto, hospitals' rights to enforce acceleration provisions of their managed care organizations contracts in effect as of June 30, 2010. Further, the facts did not sufficiently support a claimed violation of 42 C.F.R. § 433.68. However, the hospitals' claim that the manner in which managed care organizations distributed the "pass through" of enhanced capitation payments violated federally approved State Plan amendments under 62 P.S. §§ 443.1(1.1)(ii) and 803-G survived challenge, as sufficient facts were alleged.


OUTCOME: The court overruled the DPW's preliminary objections. However, preliminary objections by the HA were sustained to three claims, leaving only one surviving claim regarding the enhanced capitation payments.

Allegheny County Deputy Sheriffs' Ass'n v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd.


No. 959 C.D. 2009


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


68 A.3d 6; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 129


April 17, 2013, Argued 
May 2, 2013, Decided 
May 2, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by, Motion dismissed by, As moot Allegheny County Deputy Sheriffs' Ass'n v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2839 (Pa., Nov. 26, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. PF-R-08-74-W. State Agency: Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board.
Allegheny County Deputy Sheriffs' Ass'n v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 615 Pa. 126, 41 A.3d 839, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 635 (2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A matter was before the court on remand from the Pennsylvania Supreme Court. Petitioner County Deputy Sheriffs' Association (Union) filed a petition with the Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board arguing that they were policemen for collective bargaining purposes under the Policemen and Firemen Collective Bargaining Act (Act 111) under § 2162 of the Municipal Police Education and Training Law (MPETL) and 18 Pa.C.S. § 103.


OVERVIEW: The Board found that the Union was certified as the exclusive representative of the deputy sheriffs' collective bargaining unit and that the deputy sheriffs' primary duties related to County's court operations, including providing court security, serving process for the courts, executing warrants for the courts and transporting prisoners for the courts. The Board found they were not police officers under Act 111. The court found that the deputy sheriffs were not police officers within the meaning of Act 111. The Pennsylvania Supreme Court reversed, holding that the Board erred in applying the two-pronged test in determining that the deputy sheriffs were not police officers within the purview of Act 111. However, the Supreme Court also acknowledged that the trial court had not considered the County's constitutional claims. On review, the court held the deputy sheriffs would be improperly singled out for disparate treatment, as a sub-classification for collective bargaining purposes, from all other deputy sheriffs in the Commonwealth based on the provisions in the Crimes Code and the MPETL when the Constitution made no such distinction and such disparate treatment was prohibited.


OUTCOME: The Board's order was affirmed.

 

1704 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Office of the Lieutenant Governor v. Mohn


No. 1167 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


67 A.3d 123; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 125


February 13, 2013, Argued 
April 24, 2013, Decided 
April 24, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. AP 2012-0787. State Agency: Office of Open Records.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, the Office of the Pennsylvania Lieutenant Governor (LG) (OLG), petitioned for review of a final determination of the Pennsylvania Office of Open Records (OOR) directing the OLG to disclose to respondent, a requester, the home address of an OLG employee and all agency-issued e-mail addresses for the LG under the Pennsylvania Right to Know Law (RTKL), 2008 Pa. Laws 6, 65 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 67.101 et seq.


OVERVIEW: The reviewing court held that there was no constitutional privacy right under Pa. Const. art. I, §§ 1 and 8 in one's home address. The current RTKL's personal identification information (PII) exception under § 708(b)(1)(ii) (65 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 67.708(b)(1)(ii)) of the RTKL did not require the OOR to balance personal security interests against the benefits of disclosure. The balancing test used under the prior RTKL did not apply. The affidavit of the OLG's chief security officer did not prove that home addresses should be added to the three items of personal information that were reasonably likely to result in identity theft and fraud. The OLG did not show that the disclosure of an individual's home address would be reasonably likely to result in a substantial and demonstrable risk of harm to the personal security of the employee. The LG's secondary, government-issued e-mail addresses were exempt from disclosure under the PII exception, § 708(b)(6)(i)(A) (§ 67.708(b)(6)(i)(A)) of the RTKL, even though they were used to conduct agency business, as they were personal to the LG. The requester could request e-mails from that account if they were not exempt from disclosure.


OUTCOME: The final determination was affirmed to the extent that it provided access to the home address of an OLG employee, but reversed as to the portion providing for the disclosure of all agency-issued e-mail addresses for the LG.

1706 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Ward v. Commonwealth


No. 1452 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


65 A.3d 1078; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 113


December 28, 2012, Submitted 
April 18, 2013, Decided 
April 18, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2011-7702 CIVIL. Common Pleas Court of the County of Cumberland. Ebert, Jr., J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee Department of Transportation, Bureau of Motor Vehicles (DOT) suspended the automobile registration of appellant constable for his failure to pay the $36 registration fee. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Cumberland (Pennsylvania) affirmed the suspension. The constable appealed.


OVERVIEW: The constable claimed that under 75 Pa.C.S. § 1901(a), he was exempt as a governmental or quasi-governmental entity from paying the registration fee. The court held that a constable was not a governmental or quasi-governmental entity. A constable, created by statute, had no authority to act on behalf of the government unit in which he worked. 44 Pa.C.S. § 7142(e) expressly provided that a county was not to be liable for the acts of a constable through the doctrine of respondeat superior. The constable had no authority to register a vehicle on behalf of the political subdivision in which he worked. He bought the vehicle on his own and determined on his own that he would maintain it solely for work. Furthermore, because the constable was not entitled to the fee exemption, the exemption could not be an emolument of office under Pa. Const. art. III, § 27. Finally, the DOT was not equitably estopped from requesting a registration fee and from revoking the plate. The trial court found that given the constable's testimony that he considered certain equipment necessary to perform his duties, he would have purchased the equipment in any event.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Commonwealth v. Real Prop. & Improvements at 2338 N. Beechwood St.


No. 631 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


65 A.3d 1055; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 93


February 13, 2013, Argued 
April 5, 2013, Decided 
April 5, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, Vacated by, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Real Prop. & Improvements at 2338 N. Beechwood St., 2015 Pa. LEXIS 1098 (Pa., May 20, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. CP-51-MD-0008785-2011. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Patrick, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant property owner (PO) sought review of an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania), which granted a forfeiture petition by appellee, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, pursuant to the Controlled Substances Forfeiture Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 6801-6802. The trial court ordered the forfeiture of the PO's personal residence.


OVERVIEW: The Commonwealth filed its forfeiture petition against the PO's property, seeking forfeiture because, inter alia, drug activity had occurred there, drugs and money were seized from the residence, and arrests were made pursuant to the Controlled Substance, Drug, Device and Cosmetic Act, 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 780-101 - 780-144. After a hearing, the trial court granted the forfeiture request. It found that the PO failed to meet her burden under 42 Pa.C.S. § 6802(j) of showing that she was an innocent owner. On appeal, the court noted that the PO was indigent and had appeared at the forfeiture hearing pro se. Moreover, she was not informed that she had a right to a jury trial at any stage of the proceedings before the trial court. Although there was a jury trial right under Pa. Const. art. I, § 6, the trial court found that the PO waived that right under Pa.R.C.P. No. 1007.1(a). However, the court disagreed and held that Rule 1007.1 did not apply, as the due process concerns were not satisfied. Rather, any such jury trial waiver had to be knowing, intelligent, and on the record, as required under Pa. R. Crim. P. 620. As there was no such waiver, the matter could not proceed.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order of the trial court and remanded the matter for a new hearing.

Meade v. City of Philadelphia


No. 1995 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


65 A.3d 1031; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 68


October 17, 2012, Argued 
March 20, 2013, Decided 
March 20, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. December Term, 2010, No. 258. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Tereshko, J.
Meade v. City of Phila., 2012 Phila. Ct. Com. Pl. LEXIS 14 (2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant City of Philadelphia sought review of the grant of summary judgment by the Court of Common Pleas of Philadelphia County (Pennsylvania) in favor of two members of the Board of Revision of Taxes (BRT). The members of the BRT claimed that they were public officers within the meaning of Pa. Const. art. III, § 27 and that the City had no authority to reduce their salaries until the completion of their current terms of office.


OVERVIEW: The BRT members agreed to relinquish their real property assessment responsibilities. Pursuant to a Memorandum of Understanding and executed by the Mayor's Office of the City, the Finance Director's Office of the City, and the BRT, responsibility for assessments would be vested in the Finance Director or his designee. The members of the BRT sought to enjoin the referendum. The BRT members then filed suit contending that the City lacked authority to eliminate the BRT's appellate function and also complained about the reduction in salary. The appellate court agreed with the trial court that the members were public officers within the meaning of Pa. Const. art. III, § 27. The members of the BRT, even when stripped of their authority to perform assessments, met the criteria set forth in Richie. Their duties were set by statute and the members served for a set term. Further, the Pennsylvania Supreme Court held that the BRT's duties were quasi-judicial in character. Additionally, the BRT's services were an essential part of the real estate taxation system. Whether it made sense to prohibit a mid-term reduction in salary when duties were reduced, was not for the Court to determine.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

1709 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Weissenberger v. Chester County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 157 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


62 A.3d 501; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 61


December 12, 2012, Argued 
March 8, 2013, Decided 
March 8, 2013, Filed


NOTICE: PUBLISHED IN TABLE FORMAT IN THE ATLANTIC REPORTER.


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Weissenberger v. Chester County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2194 (Pa., Sept. 26, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2003-09496-AB. Common Pleas Court of the County of Chester. Gavin, Senior Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In tax assessment appeals raising constitutional challenges, a School District sought review of the decision of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania) which reversed an increase in the assessed value of two parcels based on a determination that the School District intentionally and selectively exercised its right to appeal, thereby violating the taxpayer's entitlement to uniform treatment.


OVERVIEW: A taxpayer owned an apartment complex situated on two parcels of property located in the School District. The School District contested the property assessments. The Board increased the value of each parcel. The taxpayer's properties were the only potentially under-assessed properties recommended for appeal by the School District. That was because of the size of the potential under-assessment. Given that recommendation, it was difficult to give much weight to the taxpayer's "arbitrary and capricious selection" argument. However, it was easy to envision a rational basis for the School District taking the appeals. Judicious use of resources to legally increase revenue was a legitimate governmental purpose. Additionally, the School District proved that the taxpayer's properties were in fact under-assessed. The taxpayer, however, did not prove that any other property was under-assessed. Thus, the taxpayer failed to prove that other properties were situated similarly to its properties. The trial court erred in concluding that the School District's decision to appeal only the taxpayer's assessments constituted deliberate, purposeful discrimination in violation of the Uniformity Clause.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed.

MCT Transp. v. Phila. Parking Auth.


No. 481 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


60 A.3d 899; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 47


October 17, 2012, Argued 
February 14, 2013, Decided 
February 14, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at Phila. Reg'l Limousine Ass'n v. Phila. Parking Auth., 2013 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 132 (Feb. 14, 2013)
Affirmed by MCT Transp. Inc. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 623 Pa. 417, 83 A.3d 85, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2793 (Nov. 20, 2013)
Affirmed by MCT Transp. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 622 Pa. 741, 81 A.3d 813, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2788 (Nov. 20, 2013)
Related proceeding at Z&R Cab, LLC v. Phila. Parking Auth., 22 F. Supp. 3d 498, 2014 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 76565 (E.D. Pa., June 4, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
McT Transp. v. Phila. Parking Auth., 2012 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 982 (July 10, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent parking authority filed a motion to dismiss the petition filed by petitioners, a group of taxicab companies, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief. Petitioners filed a motion for summary relief. Petitioners' suit challenged the Parking Authority Law, 53 Pa.C.S. § 5707(b), as being unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners contended that § 5707(b) was unconstitutional because it did not impose any limitations on defendant's exercise of discretion in its activities of setting its annual budget and fee schedule. Petitioners asserted that § 5707(b) effected a taking without due process of law because it deprived them of the opportunity for a hearing on the amount of licensing fees they must pay to defendant in order to stay in business and that the fees were excessive and confiscatory. The court held that § 5707(b) was unconstitutional because the Pennsylvania General Assembly failed to establish standards directing respondent's exercise of discretion in deciding how much to spend each year to regulate common carriers providing taxicab and limousine service in Philadelphia. Additionally, the General Assembly failed to give respondent standards to guide or restrain it in setting fees in any fashion whatsoever. Because § 5707(b) lacks standards to guide the establishment of an annual budget and fee schedule, the court held that it delegated legislative power to respondent, in violation of separation of powers.


OUTCOME: The court overruled respondent's preliminary objections and granted, in part, petitioners' motion for summary relief by declaring the particular section of the Parking Authority Law at issue unconstitutional and unenforceable. The court enjoined respondent from taking any action with regard to petitioners under the statute. The court denied the remainder of petitioners' motion.

Caba v. Weaknecht


No. 318 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


64 A.3d 39; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 40
