February 4, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by, En banc, Reconsideration denied by, En banc Caba v. Weaknecht, 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 77 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Mar. 27, 2013)
Appeal denied by Caba v. Weaknecht, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2460 (Pa., Oct. 23, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2011-21884. Common Pleas Court of the County of Berks. Sprecher, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant firearm licensee sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Berks (Pennsylvania), which overruled and denied his appeal from a decision of appellee, a county sheriff. The sheriff had revoked the licensee's concealed carry license pursuant to 18 Pa.C.S. § 6109(e)(1)(i) of the Pennsylvania Uniform Firearms Act of 1995, 18 Pa.C.S. §§ 6101-6127.


OVERVIEW: The sheriff previously issued a license to the licensee. Two years later, the sheriff revoked the license upon finding that the licensee was a "person whose character/reputation indicated danger to public safety." The licensee filed an appeal with the trial court. An evidentiary hearing on the merits was held, whereupon the trial court denied the appeal. I, §§ 1 and 11, and the revocation notice was inadequate, he failed to establish demonstrable prejudice such that relief was not warranted. Further, the exigency of the situation allowed for no pre-deprivation hearing, and the trial court's hearing cured the lack of a post-deprivation hearing. Lastly, § 6109(e)(1)(i) did not violate the Equal Protection Clause of amend. XIV.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the trial court.

Livny v. Unemployment Comp. Bd. of Review


No. 1498 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


60 A.3d 594; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 34; Unemployment Ins. Rep. (CCH) P12,654


December 28, 2012, Submitted 
January 29, 2013, Decided 
January 29, 2013, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. B-536686. State Agency: Unemployment Compensation Board of Review.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant, proceeding pro se, petitioned for review of an order of the Unemployment Compensation Board of Review (UCBR) affirming the decision of a referee to deny claimant unemployment compensation benefits. The UCBR found that claimant was ineligible for benefits because claimant's work for a Jewish day school (employer) did not constitute "employment" under the Unemployment Compensation Law, 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. §753(l)(4)(8)(a)(ii).


OVERVIEW: The definition of employment did not include service performed for certain religious organizations. Because the second prong of the statute, which required an organization to be operated or controlled by a church, had previously been declared unconstitutional, the commonwealth court limited its inquiry to whether the employer was operated primarily for religious purposes so that the claimant's work as a cook at the school would not be considered "employment." Substantial evidence in the record supported the UCBR's conclusion that the employer operated primarily for religious purposes. At the hearing, the employer presented both testimonial and documentary evidence describing the employer's religious mission and curriculum. The head of the school testified that the employer was a tax-exempt religious organization under the Internal Revenue Code and that the employer was "formed with the intent of being a religious organization with the mission of providing a richly fortified Jewish education to its students. Finally, the employer was registered with the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania as a non-profit religious organization.


OUTCOME: The commonwealth court affirmed.

Friends of Pa. Leadership Charter Sch. v. Chester County Bd. of Assessment Appeals

December 12, 2012, Argued 
January 16, 2013, Decided

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a charter school, challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Chester County (Pennsylvania), which granted the motion for judgment on the pleadings filed by appellees, a county board of assessment (board) and a school district in the charter school's appeal from the board's denial of its claim for a refund of real estate taxes paid to the county.

Berkhimer v. State Emples. Ret. Bd.


No. 2031 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


60 A.3d 873; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 20


October 15, 2012, Argued 
January 14, 2013, Decided 
January 14, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Berkhimer v. State Employees' Ret. Bd., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2108 (Pa., Sept. 18, 2013)
Appeal denied by Berkhimer v. State Emples. Ret. Bd., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2098 (Pa., Sept. 18, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2009-17. State Agency: State Employees' Retirement Board.
In re Berkhimer, 593 Pa. 366, 930 A.2d 1255, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 1677 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, former judge, sought review of the order of respondent, the State Employees' Retirement Board (Board), which affirmed the decision of the State Employees' Retirement System forfeiting his entire accrued pension benefit following his removal from the office of magisterial district judge by the Pennsylvania Court of Judicial Discipline.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was removed from office for inappropriate comments of a sexual nature made toward court staff. On appeal, petitioner asserted that the forfeiture of his pension violated various laws, including ex post facto, due process, and the prohibition of the diminishment of judicial compensation in violation of Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(a). The court disagreed and held that Pa. Const. art. V, § 18, Pa. Const. art. VI, and the Judicial Code, 42 Pa.C.S. § 3352(a), were clear and are not dependent on how or where the misconduct occurred and simply provided for the forfeiture of compensation and retirement benefits where a judge or magisterial district judge is removed from office for any violation of Pa. Const. art. V, § 18. The court noted that the forfeiture provision of Pa. Const. art. V, § 16(b) was mandatory once a judge has been removed from office. The court did find that the Board improperly forfeited petitioner's pension attributable to his military service credit, since that service credit was not earned via his employment with the Commonwealth.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the Board insofar as it directed the forfeiture of that part of petitioner's pension attributable to his military service credit. Otherwise, the court affirmed the Board's order directing the forfeiture of the remainder of petitioner's pension attributable to his state service as a magisterial district judge.


 

1715 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Berks County Tax Collection Comm. v. Pa. Dep't of Cmty. & Econ. Dev.


No. 378 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


60 A.3d 589; 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 8


December 7, 2012, Submitted 
January 7, 2013, Decided 
January 7, 2013, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Berks County Tax Collection Comm. v. Pa. Dep't of Cmty. & Econ. Dev., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2807 (Pa., Nov. 21, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, multiple county tax collection committees (TCCs), filed a motion under Pa.R.A.P. 1532(b) for summary relief against respondent, the Pennsylvania Department of Community and Economic Development (DCED), wherein they sought a declaratory judgment regarding how to apply an earned income tax (EIT) credit under § 317 of the Local Tax Enabling Act, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 6924.317, in particular circumstances.


OVERVIEW: Section 6924.317 had been interpreted to provide for a "super credit" that allowed a non-resident of Philadelphia who earned income there to apply an EIT credit to a non-Philadelphia school district or other political subdivision's imposition of an EIT. This was based on payments by the non-resident under § 1(a) of the Sterling Act, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 15971. The TCCs sought a declaration that such application was incorrect, and a directive that an "apportionment" application of § 6924.317 was proper. In response, the DCED indicated that it had not determined whether to a "super credit" or an "apportionment" application was more appropriate due to lack of clarity in the law. The court noted that in a prior opinion to the former version of § 6924.317, it had approved use of the "super credit." As the legislature's amendment to that statutory section did not change the language in that regard, the "super credit" was still properly employed. The TCCs' challenge under the Uniformity Clause of Pa. Const. art. 8, § 1 failed, as taxpayers who earned income entirely outside of Philadelphia, and those who earned it both inside and outside of Philadelphia, were not similarly situated.


OUTCOME: The court denied the motion for summary relief.

Streck v. Lower Macungie Twp. Bd. of Comm'rs


No. 1804 C.D. 2011, No. 1809 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

December 14, 2012, Decided 
December 14, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by, As moot, Application granted by, Remanded by Streck v. Lower Macungie Twp. Bd. of Comm'rs, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 1004 (Pa., May 17, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2010-C-4036. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lehigh. Varricchio, J.
Streck v. Lower Macungie Twp. Bd. of Comm'rs, 2011 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 274 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Lehigh County (Pennsylvania) that invalidated the 2010 amendments to the Zoning Ordinance of Lower Macungie Township (Pennsylvania). The trial court did so because it found that the 2010 amendments had not been enacted in accordance with the public notice requirements for a zoning ordinance.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that trial court erred in finding that the township's pre-enactment notices of the ordinance at issue did not comply with § 610 of the Pennsylvania Municipalities Planning Code, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 10610, because the township's summary listed the salient details of the ordinance in "reasonable" detail. The summary gave the reader notice that there would be a major rezoning of land located within the township, and it advised any citizen interested in more information on where to obtain a free copy of the new zoning map and the complete text of the ordinance. Further, the court rejected the objectors' claim that the ordinance should be nullified because it was an illegal zoning contract.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order.

Johnson v. Allegheny Intermediate Unit


No. 789 C.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


59 A.3d 10; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 329


September 12, 2012, Argued 
December 13, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. GD 12-3329. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. O'Reilly, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state education department challenged an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Allegheny County (Pennsylvania) that permanently enjoined an employer from terminating an employee's employment pursuant to 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1-111(e)(1).


OVERVIEW: The trial court erred in determining that an employee's termination under 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1-111(e)(1) violated the Ex Post Facto Clause of the Pennsylvania Constitution where, inter alia, the statute's purpose was not to punish those convicted of the enumerated offenses, and the lifetime ban was clearly rationally related to a non-punitive purpose. However, because 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1-111(e)(1) created a lifetime ban for a homicide offense that had no temporal proximity to the employee's present ability to perform the duties of his position and did not bear a real and substantial relationship to the Commonwealth's interest in protecting children, it was unreasonable, unduly oppressive, and patently beyond the necessities of the offense. As a result, it imposed unusual and unnecessary restrictions upon the employee's lawful employment as a fatherhood facilitator with the employer and was unconstitutional as violative of his substantive due process rights as guaranteed by Pa. Const. art. 1, § 1.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed, although on different grounds.

Breeden v. Borough of Crafton


No. 2300 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


57 A.3d 222; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 327


November 13, 2012, Argued 
December 4, 2012, Decided 
December 4, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. SA 11-000582. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. Judge Della Vecchia, J.
Breeden v. Borough of Crafton, 2010 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 481 (2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant former police officer challenged the order of the Allegheny County Common Pleas Court (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a decision of appellee borough denying his appeal from a Pension Plan Administrator's (Plan Administrator's) decision that he was not entitled to a vested pension.


OVERVIEW: The former police officer was terminated from his job with the borough due to his involvement in a motor vehicle/pedestrian accident that resulted in a death. The borough determined that his conduct constituted conduct unbecoming an officer, a violation of the law, neglect or violation of his official duties, and disobedience of orders. Following his termination, the Plan Administrator for the borough's police pension denied the former police officer's request to have his pension vested due to his employment termination for cause. The trial court upheld that decision, finding that the Plan Administrator properly denied the request to have the former police officer's pension vested under the terms of the parties' collective bargaining agreement (CBA). The court found no error, concluding that the former police officer had notice, based on the CBA, that if he was discharged for cause he would also lose his pension. Accordingly, the trial court did not err in determining that the borough could retroactively void his pension benefits for events which occurred after the date his pension benefits vested.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

 

1719 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Commonwealth v. 605 University Drive


No. 789 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


61 A.3d 1048; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 321


March 13, 2012, Argued 
November 21, 2012, Decided 
November 21, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal granted by, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. All That Certain Lot or Parcel of Land Located at 605 Univ. Drive, 620 Pa. 584, 71 A.3d 915, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 1515 (2013)
Reversed by, Judgment entered by Commonwealth v. All That Certain Lot Or Parcel of Land Located at 605 Univ. Drive, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 3026 (Pa., Nov. 19, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. CP-14-MD-1134-2009. Common Pleas Court of the County of Centre. Grine, President Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Centre County (Pennsylvania) which granted the Office of Attorney General's (Commonwealth) motion for summary judgment in a proceeding under what was commonly known as the Controlled Substances Forfeiture Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 6801-6802.


OVERVIEW: Defendant filed an answer to the Commonwealth's forfeiture petition admitting that he was the owner of the property "on paper," but stating that for all intents and purposes the owner was his mother. He also denied that the property was used or intended to be used for drug trafficking. He ultimately pled guilty to multiple drug charges and was sentenced to a term of incarceration. The trial court granted the Commonwealth's motion for summary judgment and ordered the property forfeited to the Commonwealth. The appellate court held that the Pennsylvania Rules of Civil Procedure did not apply to forfeitures because the General Assembly provided the complete procedure to be followed that preempted the area. A forfeiture proceeding did not fall under Pa.R.C.P. No. 206.1(a)(1). Because a forfeiture proceeding was a quasi-criminal punitive proceeding, the General Assembly mandated a hearing requiring the Commonwealth to present its evidence in open court, much like it had to do in a criminal proceeding where similar constitutional principles were implicated and not do it just on the papers.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was reversed. The matter was remanded to the trial court to conduct a hearing as required by § 6802(i) of the Forfeiture Act.

Commonwealth v. Jackson

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. CP-51-MD-0008744-2009. Common Pleas Court of the County of Philadelphia. Judge Palumbo, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate pro se challenged the order of the (Jackson) appeals pro se from the Philadelphia County Court of Common Pleas (Pennsylvania), which directed that $ 8,603 in United States currency be forfeited to the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania (Commonwealth), pursuant to the Controlled Substances Forfeitures Act, 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 6801-6802.


OVERVIEW: The money at issue was seized from appellant after he was stopped by police officers for failing to stop his vehicle at a red light. On appeal, appellant argued that the trial court violated his rights by ordering forfeiture of his property after he was acquitted of the drug possession charges. The court disagreed and noted that it has already been determined that the fact that appellant was acquitted of the underlying criminal charges did not preclude the trial court from ordering forfeiture of the money. However, the court did find that the trial court erred by denying appellant the opportunity to be present at the forfeiture hearing. The court found that there was no record evidence that once appellant was incarcerated, the Commonwealth properly notified him of the forfeiture hearing, that he waived his right to attend, or that arrangements could not be made to insure his attendance at the hearing. Thus, appellant was not properly notified of his right to arrange his attendance at the forfeiture hearing.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order and remanded the case to the trial court for further proceedings.

Pa. State Ass'n of Jury Comm'rs v. Commonwealth


No. 21 M.D. 2012


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


53 A.3d 109; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 219


April 18, 2012, Argued 
July 26, 2012, Decided 
July 26, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by Pa. State Ass'n of Jury Comm'rs v. Commonwealth, 64 A.3d 611, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 458 (Pa., 2013)
Related proceeding at, Application granted by, Injunction denied by, Judgment entered by Pa. State Ass'n of Jury Comm'rs v. Commonwealth, 2013 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 296 (Pa. Commw. Ct., July 31, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In an original jurisdiction matter, intervenors, two county commissioner associations, filed motions for a judgment on the pleadings in the suit brought by petitioners, individual jury commissions. Petitioners sought declaratory and injunctive relief relating to Act 108, which amended the County Code, 16 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 102(a), 401(f), and 1805(b) (Act 108). Petitioners filed a competing motion.


OVERVIEW: Act 108 abolished the office of jury commissioner in certain counties and made other changes to their duties and powers. The court determined that 108 did not violate any of the constitutional provisions upon which petitioners relied in seeking to obtain declaratory and injunctive relief. The court held that the unifying topic of county commissioners' powers was one that could be reasonable drawn and the two substantive provisions of Act 108 bore a logical connection to that topic, therefore, Act 108 did not violate Pa. Const. art. III, § 3. Further, the court held that Act 108 did not violate the separation of powers doctrine because there was no support in the law for the proposition that the elected office of jury commissioner was part of the judicial system because the courts have no constitutional authority to exercise control over that statutorily-created office. As a result, since there were no disputes regarding the facts, the court granted intervenor's motion for judgment on the pleadings.


OUTCOME: The court granted intervenors' motions for a judgment on the pleadings and denied petitioners' motion.

Robinson Twp. v. Commonwealth

52 A.3d 463; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 222; 181 Oil & Gas Rep. 66; 42 ELR 20158

July 26, 2012, Filed

Affirmed in part and reversed in part by, Vacated by, in part, Remanded by, in part Robinson Twp. v. Commonwealth, 623 Pa. 564, 83 A.3d 901, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 3068 (2013)
Application denied by Robinson Twp. v. Commonwealth, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 1843 (Pa., Aug. 20, 2015)

Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Robinson Twp. v. Commonwealth, 2012 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 387 (2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Commonwealth of Pennsylvania, the Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission, and others, filed preliminary objections in response to a petition for review filed by petitioner municipalities. Petitioners challenged the constitutionality of Act 13, 58 Pa.C.S. §§ 2301-3504 (Act 13), regarding oil and gas operations. Cross-motions for summary relief were also before the court.


OVERVIEW: The court first addressed the standing of the parties and held that petitioners had standing to bring the action because Act 13 imposed substantial, direct and immediate obligations on them that affect their government functions and Act 13 required that the municipalities enact zoning ordinances to comply with the provisions of Act 13. OUTCOME: The court sustained, in part, the preliminary objections filed by the Commonwealth, overruled petitioners' motion for summary relief, in part, declared several portions of Act 13 unconstitutional, null and void, and denied respondents' cross-motion for summary relief.

Sears v. Corbett


No. 121 M.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


49 A.3d 463; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 183


December 14, 2011, Argued 
June 27, 2012, Decided 
June 27, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Companion case at Weisblatt v. Corbett, 53 A.3d 91, 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 190 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2012)
Reversed by, Vacated by Sears v. Wolf, 2015 Pa. LEXIS 1342 (Pa., June 19, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, former insurance recipients, filed a class action against respondent lawmakers seeking declaratory, mandamus, and equitable relief with respect to the lawmakers' alleged violations of the Tobacco Settlement Act, 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 5701.101-.5103, and the Pennsylvania Constitution. The lawmakers filed preliminary objections to the former recipients' second amended complaint.


OVERVIEW: The former recipients participated in a low-cost health insurance program, which was funded by the proceeds of a Master Settlement Agreement (MSA) between certain tobacco product manufacturers, the Commonwealth, and 46 other states. The lawmakers enacted legislation redirecting settlement monies received under the MSA from the tobacco settlement account and a related health endowment account to the Commonwealth's General Fund. As a result, the insurance program lacked necessary funding and ceased operations. The doctrine of sovereign immunity barred the mandamus relief sought because the lawmakers would have been required to take affirmative action, including the enactment of new legislation, in order to effectuate such relief. Furthermore, to the extent that the former recipients sought to restrain the lawmakers from enacting future legislation affecting the settlement monies, relief was barred by the Speech and Debate Clause of Pa. Const. art. II, § 15. Nonetheless, the former recipients stated sufficient claims challenging the constitutionality of the legislation under 35 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 5701.1303 and the Pennsylvania Constitution.


OUTCOME: The court sustained in part and overruled in part the lawmakers' preliminary objections.

DoMiJo, LLC v. McLain

April 18, 2012, Filed

Appealed from No. 2011-CV-1877. Common Pleas Court of the County of Lackawanna. O'Brien, Senior Judge.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant objectors challenged the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Lackawanna (Pennsylvania), which determined that appellee, a township zoning hearing board (ZHB), erred by denying appellee applicant's request for a certificate of nonconforming use and determining that a legal nonconforming use existed. The objectors also appealed the denial of their motion to quash.


OVERVIEW: The applicant had purchased the property at issue in 2007 but failed to re-register the certificate of nonconforming use obtained by the prior owner within 60 days of purchasing the property, as required by a township ordinance. The ZHB denied the applicant's request for a certificate due to its failure to timely re-register the same under the ordinance. The court found that the trial court did not err in denying the objectors? motion to quash the applicant's appeal because the applicant's notice of appeal sufficiently placed the parties and the trial court on notice of the issue on appeal and its grounds. Further, the court determined that the trial court correctly found that the ZHB erred in concluding that the applicant was not entitled to continue the nonconforming use because it failed to timely re-register as the right to continue a nonconforming use arises from constitutional protections and not from regulatory provisions, thus, the right could not be lost in that way. Further, the plain language of the ordinance did not require such a result, as it did not disclose the consequences for a property owner's failure to timely re-register its nonconforming use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

R & R Express v. Commonwealth


No. 533 F.R. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA

February 8, 2012, Decided 
February 8, 2012, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 0613374. State Agency Board of Finance and Revenue.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayer sought review of the order of the Board of Finance and Revenue which sustained the assessment of additional tax and interest under the Motor Carriers Road Tax Act (Act), 75 Pa.C.S. §§ 9601-9622, following an audit of the taxpayer. Because the taxpayer purchased all fuel at retail, it contended that the Board erred in failing to provide it with any credit for taxes paid on the estimated fuel use.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer hauled steel and other commodities throughout the United States by soliciting loads from various entities. It then hired owner operators, many of which were signed to permanent leases and issued International Fuel Tax Agreement (IFTA) decals, to haul the loads. The court held that while the taxpayer's approach, that of using available information and audit techniques to arrive at an estimate of taxes paid on fuel consumed, had a logical appeal, it was completely contrary to the tax scheme established by the Act and IFTA. The court also rejected any suggestion that an unconstitutional taxation has occurred. There was no statutory or regulatory authority to support application of post-audit period data to calculate tax due.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Board was affirmed.


 

1729 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Barrel of Monkeys, LLC v. Allegheny County


No. 424 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


39 A.3d 559; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 57


November 14, 2011, Argued 
February 8, 2012, Decided 
February 8, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. GD 07-027730. Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny. O'Brien, A.J.
Lawrenceville Brewery, Inc. v. Allegheny County (In re Barrel of Monkeys, LLC), 2011 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 222 (2011)
Barrel of Monkeys v. Allegheny County, 2011 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 219 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In accordance with 53 Pa.C.S. § 8602, appellee County passed an ordinance known as the "Drink Tax." Appellant liquor-licensed restaurants brought an action challenging the Drink Tax. The Common Pleas Court of the County of Allegheny (Pennsylvania) held that the Drink Tax was valid. It, therefore granted the County's motion for summary judgment and denied the restaurants' cross-motion. The restaurants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Section 8602 authorized second class counties to obtain support for transit systems by imposing a tax on the sale at retail of liquor and malt and brewed beverages within the county. The restaurants argued that two separate enactments were required when taxing one industry and directing that money to a separate and distinct industry; otherwise, legislation ran afoul of the single subject rule of Pa. Const. art. III, § 3. However, the use of the money raised by the § 8602-enabled Drink Tax was unquestionably germane to the overarching subject of that enactment, transportation. Furthermore, the Drink Tax did not violate the uniformity clause of Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1 or the equal protection clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV. Because liquor licensed restaurants and state stores and distributors were taxed under different statutory regimes, the trial court correctly concluded that the County had a legitimate and rational basis for their different treatment with regard to the Drink Tax. Finally, the conflict-of-interest provisions of the County Administrative Code did not empower the trial court to void a vote by a member of County Council who may have been ineligible to vote.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Bornstein v. City of Connellsville


No. 150 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


39 A.3d 513; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 45; 33 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 787


November 15, 2011, Argued 
February 2, 2012, Decided 
February 2, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 3024 of 2008. G.D. Common Pleas Court of the County of Fayette. Leskinen, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a former health officer, challenged the order of the Court of Common Pleas of Fayette County (Pennsylvania), which affirmed the decision of appellee city council terminating her from her position as the city's health officer. A hearing was held before the city council.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania State Ethics Commission had found that the former health officer had conducted business for other municipalities while being paid by the city and used the city's office equipment and supplies in doing so. The former health officer's primary contention on appeal was that the city council violated her procedural due process rights by failing to give her adequate notice of the hearing addressing her termination. The court found that a letter from the city council advising of the hearing gave less than three business days' notice. The court held that the time of the notice in the case was plainly inadequate, especially when considering the substantial property interest at stake and the relatively minor burdens of providing notice. As a result, the court held that the city council violated the former health officer's due process rights by failing to give her notice sufficiently in advance of the hearing. The court found no issue existed with regard to the city's director of public safety and health participating in the hearing since it was not an impermissible commingling of the prosecutorial and adjudicatory functions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to remand the matter to the city council for a new hearing.


 

1731 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Crews v. City of Chester


No. 2479 C.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


35 A.3d 1267; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 36


October 18, 2011, Argued 
January 25, 2012, Decided 
January 25, 2012, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 03-6782. Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Proud, J.
Crews v. City of Chester, 983 A.2d 829, 2009 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 1554 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, a minor and his mother, appealed the orders of the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania), which denied their motion for summary judgment, dismissed their complaint against defendant city, and entered summary judgment in the city's favor. Plaintiffs brought a declaratory judgment action challenging a city ordinance and sought damages for personal injuries and emotional distress suffered by the minor.


OVERVIEW: The minor was arrested under a city ordinance prohibiting loitering. Plaintiffs asserted in their complaint that the ordinance was unconstitutional and sought damages for medical and legal expenses incurred following the minor's arrest and holding at the police station for approximately one hour until his mother arrived. The court found that the ordinance was not facially overbroad but agreed with plaintiffs that it was impermissibly vague by offering no guidance as to what constituted a lawful and reasonable explanation for a person's presence in a high drug activity area or what a person must do to comply with a police dispersal order. As a result, the court found that the trial court erred by granting summary judgment in the city's favor on that issue. Further, the court found that the city applied the ordinance in a manner that violated them minor's Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, rights and, thus, erred by granting the city summary judgment on that issue as well.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order to the extent that it granted summary judgment in favor of the city on the vagueness and Fourth Amendment claims and ordered that summary judgment be entered in favor of plaintiffs as to those claims only. The court otherwise affirmed the order in all other respects.

 

1732 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Pa. Workers' Comp. Judges P.A. v. Exec. Bd. of Pa.


No. 932 M.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


39 A.3d 486; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 32


April 5, 2011, Argued 
January 19, 2012, Decided 
January 19, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed without opinion by Pa. Workers' Comp. Judges v. Exec. Bd. of Pa., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 1008 (Pa., May 21, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Executive Board of Pennsylvania and the Department of Labor and Industry, filed preliminary objections to a petition for review filed by petitioners, the Workers' Compensation Judges Professional Association and related others, alleging that rescission of a promised lump sum payment for salary purposes violated the Contracts and Taking Clauses of the United States and Pennsylvania Constitutions, and 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983.


OVERVIEW: The workers' compensation judges were classified as management level employees and accordingly, were not represented by a union and did not engage in collective bargaining. Due to budgetary concerns, the Board rescinded a one-step longevity increment, or lump sum payment, that had been approved for the judges. They filed suit, alleging that the rescission violated constitutional and statutory provisions and seeking both declaratory and injunctive relief. In resolving respondents' preliminary objections, the court found that as individuals sued in their official capacity were not "persons" for purposes of § 1983, those claims could not stand. As the longevity increment was not bargained for and there was no consideration, there was no enforceable contract right. Further, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Pa. Const. art. I, § 17 did not prohibit the abrogation of the salary of a public officer during the term of his office. Additionally, the Board's resolutions did not constitute legislative actions. The claims under the Takings Clause of U.S. Const. amend. V and Pa. Const. art. I, § 10 also failed, as the judges had no property right in increased salaries.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the preliminary objections and dismissed the complaint.


 

1733 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Marin v. Sec'y of Pa.


No. 375 M.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


41 A.3d 913; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 104


January 9, 2012, Submitted 
January 13, 2012, Decided 
January 13, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Publication Ordered April 2, 2012.
Affirmed without opinion by Marin v. Sec'y of Pa., 2013 Pa. LEXIS 291 (Pa., Feb. 19, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the Court were the preliminary objections in the nature of a demurrer of respondents, the Secretary of the Commonwealth, the Department of Community Development, Office of Open Records (OOR) and its Executive Director, and the Bureau of Commissions, Elections and Legislation to the petition for declaratory judgment and injunction filed by petitioner a potential candidate, who contested the constitutionality of 25 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2870.


OVERVIEW: The candidate allegedly attempted to register as a candidate and asserted that someone, presumably his local election board, refused to certify him as a candidate in that election because he refused to provide his personal residence information on his nomination petition and candidate's affidavit, as required by the Pennsylvania Election Code. He intended to run for elected office again and would again refuse to provide his home address. He argued that his privacy rights were greater than the Commonwealth's right to the information. The court agreed that the OOR and its Executive Director were not necessary or appropriate parties. There was no constitutional right to privacy in one's home address under the Pennsylvania Constitution. In addition, there was a compelling reason to require candidates for elected office to provide their home address on their nomination forms as every candidate had to be qualified for the position he sought. Just as members of the general public were required to provide their home address in order to register to vote, a candidate for public office had to provide such information.


OUTCOME: The preliminary objections were sustained. The petition for review was dismissed with prejudice.

Lancaster County v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd.


No. 926 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


35 A.3d 83; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 19


December 13, 2011, Argued 
January 12, 2012, Decided 
January 12, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Lancaster County v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 2013 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 18 (2013)
Appeal denied by Lancaster County v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 74 A.3d 1032, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 2034 (Pa., 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. PERA-C-10-10-E. State Agency: Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board.
Lancaster County v. Pa. Labor Rels. Bd., 35 A.3d 73, 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 15 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner county sought review by respondent Pennsylvania Labor Relations Board (PLRB) of a hearing officer's decision that the county committed an unfair labor practice, under 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1101.1201, in violation of 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. 1101.805, by refusing to implement an interest arbitration award. The PLRB affirmed the hearing officer's decision, and the county sought judicial review.


OVERVIEW: The county decided it could treat the arbitration award as advisory, under 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1101.805, since the award would require it to raise taxes or appropriate funds. The appellate court held it was not error for the PLRB to rule that this was an unfair labor practice because implementing the award would not require a legislative enactment by county commissioners, as (1) transfers of available unencumbered funds were not legislative enactments within the meaning of 43 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1101.805 even though the county commissioners had to vote on such transfers, (2) while implementing the award would cost a significant sum, there were sufficient unreserved funds in the county's general fund to implement the award, and (3) the fact that maintaining these reserves was necessary for a good bond rating was insufficient to overcome the fact that sufficient funds were available to implement the award.


OUTCOME: The PLRB's decision was affirmed.


 

1735 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Appeal of Sullivan


No. 2190 C.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


37 A.3d 1250; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 14


May 9, 2011, Argued 
January 11, 2012, Decided 
January 11, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by In re Appeal of Sullivan, 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 98 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Mar. 22, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 06-17988. Common Pleas Court of the County of Delaware. Proud, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants taxpayers sought judicial review in the Court of Common Pleas of Delaware County (Pennsylvania) of the decision of the Delaware County, Pa., Board of Assessment Appeals (Board) denying their appeal of an assessment of their real property. The trial court reduced the assessment, and the taxpayers appealed.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayers said the assessment violated Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1. The appellate court disagreed because (1) they presented no county wide analysis, (2) their ratio studies of assessment to sales of similar neighborhood properties did not convince the trial court, which exclusively judged the credibility and weight of evidence, and (3) their evidence did not support a county wide reassessment or show willful discrimination, so they were only entitled to an assessment conforming to the tax district's common level ratio (CLR), which they received. A county wide reassessment was not required because the taxpayers (1) presented no county wide analysis, and (2) sought no such reassessment on appeal from the Board. An assessment was not void ab initio when the assessment notice did not show old and new assessment ratios, under former 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 5349(b), because the taxpayers complained of a lack of the CLR, but the statute did not require such notice, so they could not contest the assessment nunc pro tunc. They were entitled to simple interest on a refund, based on the trial court's assessment reduction, from the date of overpayment, because 53 Pa.C.S. § 8426(a) required it.


OUTCOME: The trial court's judgment was affirmed.


 

1736 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Muscarella v. Commonwealth


No. 10 F.R. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


39 A.3d 459; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 30



January 11, 2012, Decided 
January 11, 2012, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Publication Ordered January 18, 2012.
Judgment entered by, in part, Application denied by Muscarella v. Commonwealth, 2014 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 166 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Mar. 14, 2014)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner executor filed a class action petition for review of a decision of the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue, alleging that respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania failed to rebate property taxes due under Pennsylvania's Senior Citizens Property Tax and Rebate Assistance Act, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 6926.1301-6926.1313 (2006), and its predecessors. Petitioner filed a motion for class certification.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner's claim for a property tax rebate for 2009 was denied on the basis that the decedent died before January 1, 2010. He sought to certify a class comprising persons who filed valid claims for property taxes rebates for 2003 through 2008, who paid property taxes the subsequent calendar year, who were otherwise eligible to receive rebates for that year, but who died on or before December 31 of that year and did not receive a rebate. The court held that as petitioner brought the class action in the court's appellate, not equity, jurisdiction, and sought a rebate of a property tax (not a refund of taxes erroneously paid), he was not precluded from maintaining a class action seeking rebates, as long as the action otherwise met the requirements for class certification. As there were over 2000 class members, petitioner established numerosity. There were common questions of law and fact; petitioner's claims were typical of other class members' claims; the parties stipulated that petitioner's counsel could adequately represent the class; and there were no significant judicial administration concerns. The action thus met the requirements of Pa.R.C.P. No. 1702, 1708, 1709.


OUTCOME: Petitioner's motion for class certification was granted.

Caputo v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (Commonwealth)

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


34 A.3d 908; 2012 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 9


September 14, 2011, Argued 
January 5, 2012, Decided 
January 5, 2012, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. A08-1665. State Agency: Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner claimant sought review of an order of respondent, the (Pennsylvania) Workers' Compensation Appeal Board (Board), which affirmed the decision of the Workers' Compensation Judge to deny her offset review petition. At issue was the offset imposed against the claimant's benefits, pursuant to the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act, 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 71(a).


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the claimant argued that the Board erred as the offset provision in § 71(a) that reduced her compensation disability by a portion of her old age Social Security retirement benefits violated the Equal Protection Clause, Pa. Const. art. I, § 1. The court rejected the claimant's contention and held that permitting an employer to offset workers' compensation disability benefits by 50 percent of an employee's Social Security retirement benefit was reasonably related to reducing the employer's workers' compensation costs. The court held that the offset against disability benefits in § 71(a), for 50 percent of an individual's Social Security retirement benefit was reasonably related to the legitimate governmental objectives of reducing workers' compensation costs for Pennsylvania employers and encouraging Social Security beneficiaries to participate in the workforce.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

White v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (City of Pittsburgh)


No. 673 C.D. 2011


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


38 A.3d 1031; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 625


October 21, 2011, Submitted 
December 29, 2011, Decided 
December 29, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by White v. Workers' Comp. Appeal Bd. (City of Pittsburgh), 2012 Pa. LEXIS 1419 (Pa., June 27, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. A09-0837. State Agency: Workers' Compensation Appeal Board.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employee sought review of an order from respondent, the Workers' Compensation Appeal Board (Pennsylvania), which affirmed a determination of a workers' compensation judge (WCJ). The WCJ denied and dismissed the employee's petition to review her benefit offset under § 204(a) of the Workers' Compensation Act, 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 71(a), based on her receipt of old age Social Security (SS) benefits.


OVERVIEW: The employee sustained an injury in the course of her employment and she began receiving workers' compensation benefits. Due to the employee's receipt of SS old age benefits, the employer filed a notice of workers' compensation benefit offset and began taking a 50 percent of those benefits. The employee sought review, alleging that a portion of the SS benefits were attributable to her late husband's earnings or annual cost of living increases. The WCJ denied the employee's petition, finding that the employer established its entitlement to the offset. The Board affirmed that determination. On further review, the court conducted an equal protection analysis under the rational relation test. It determined that there was no violation of Pa. Const. art. I, § 1 by the "old age" offset provision under § 71(a), as it was rationally related to the purpose of the workers' compensation law. Further, the employer met its burden of showing the source of SS benefits against which it took an offset. Accordingly, the offset was not based on any of the late husband's earning SS benefits.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order of the board.

Smolsky v. Pa. Gen. Assembly


No. 513 M.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


34 A.3d 316; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 595

December 2, 2011, Decided 
December 2, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed without opinion by Smolsky v. Pa., 2012 Pa. LEXIS 1849 (Pa., Aug. 20, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY: Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.
Smolsky v. Pa. Gen. Assembly, 2011 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 364 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondents, the Department of Corrections and the Pennsylvania General Assembly (GA), filed preliminary objections under Pa.R.C.P. No. 1028(a)(4) to a petition for review of petitioner inmate, seeking declaratory judgment relief based on the allegation that § 6602(f)(1) of the Prisoner Litigation Reform Act (PLRA), 42 Pa.C.S. § 6602(f)(1), was unconstitutional due to a conflict with the Remedies Clause of Pa. Const. art. I, § 11.


OVERVIEW: The inmate filed his petition for review on behalf of all Pennsylvania inmates. He sought a declaration that § 6602(f)(1) was unconstitutional based on an alleged conflict with the Remedies Clause, as he had been identified as an "abusive litigator" and his cases had accordingly been dismissed. Respondents' request for dismissal of the petition for review under § 6602 was denied, as it was determined that it did not constitute "prison conditions litigation." Respondents thereafter filed preliminary objections. The court reflected on prior judicial precedents and noted that the abusive litigator provisions of the PLRA had not been found to violate U.S. Const. amend. V, Pa. Const. art. I, § 1, and the equal protection clauses of both constitutions. Accordingly, it found that the complaint failed to state a cause of action because there was a legitimate governmental interest in deterring frivolous lawsuits. A preliminary objection based on legislative immunity was not deemed waived although it should have been raised under Pa.R.C.P. No. 1030. Further, such claims against the GA were within the scope of legislative immunity under the Speech or Debate Clause, Pa. Const. art. II, § 15.


OUTCOME: The court sustained respondents' preliminary objections and dismissed the inmate's petition for review.

Perry v. State Civil Serv. Comm'n (Dep't of Labor & Indus.)

COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


38 A.3d 942; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 631


September 9, 2011, Submitted 
November 14, 2011, Decided 
November 14, 2011, Filed

Publication Ordered January 25, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed from No. 26422. State Agency: State Civil Service Commission.
Perry v. State Civ. Serv. Comm'n, 2011 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 919 (2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, a former Workers' Compensation Judge Manager with the Pennsylvania Department of Labor and Industry (Department), appealed the order of the Pennsylvania Civil Service Commission (Commission), which removed him from his position based on credible evidence that he violated the Department's prohibition on the possession of weapons in the workplace.


OVERVIEW: The Commission found that the credible evidence established that petitioner possessed a handgun in his office, left a handgun in his vehicle while parked on property owned or leased by the Department, and showed the handgun to his subordinate while in his vehicle. The Commission determined that petitioner's conduct violated the Department's prohibition on the possession of weapons in the workplace. The court found that the Commission's decision was based on the credible evidence of petitioner's co-workers, thus, it was supported by the evidence. The court held that petitioner's right to bear arms was not violated since that right was not unlimited and may be restricted for the good of the order of society and the protection of citizens, such as prohibiting firearms in government buildings. The court further rejected petitioner's discrimination claims because he failed in his burden of proof since the Department offered a non-discriminatory explanation for the personnel action, the violation of the weapons policy, and that decision was supported by the evidence.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order.

Related proceeding at Phila. Entm't & Dev. Partners, L.P. v. Pennsylvania Dep't of Revenue (In re Phila. Entm't & Dev. Partners, L.P.), 2016 Bankr. LEXIS 1149 (Bankr. E.D. Pa., Apr. 8, 2016)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1]

Phila. Entm't & Dev. Partners., L.P. v. City of Philadelphia, 594 Pa. 51, 934 A.2d 1158, 2007 Pa. LEXIS 2225 (2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner casino sought review of an order of respondent Pennsylvania Gaming Control Board granting summary judgment in favor of the Board's Bureau of Investigation and Enforcement (BIE) and revoking the casino's Category 2 Slot Machine License. The issues were, inter alia, whether the Board applied an incorrect legal test to determine that the casino violated conditions of its license and whether the casino's due process rights were violated.


OVERVIEW: The casino applied for a Category 2 License. The Board issued a Suitability Report which deemed the casino financially suitable and awarded a License subject to certain conditions. After receiving extensions, the Board denied the casino's motion for extension and imposed a $2,000 per diem sanction continuing until the casino complied with the conditions of the Board's order. The Board tabled a ruling on BIE's motion for summary judgment and gave the casino until a certain date to provide definitive documentation of a transaction with the casino's, and report at the Board's meeting. The Board granted summary judgment in favor of BIE, and revoked the casino's license. The court, applying 4 Pa.C.S. § 1202 among other statutes, held the Board did not commit reversible error by applying the same financial suitability test used to determine the casino's eligibility as a slot machine license applicant to determine that it violated the conditions as a licensee. The casino's due process rights were not violated as lesser sanctions had been imposed without success. Revocation was not an excessive sanction under the circumstances.


OUTCOME: The order of the board was affirmed.

1744 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. TAP Pharm. Prods.


No. 212 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


36 A.3d 1197; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 415


May 9, 2011, Argued 
August 31, 2011, Decided 
August 31, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by Commonwealth v. TAP Pharm. Prods., 626 Pa. 1, 94 A.3d 350, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 1498 (2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Commonwealth v. TAP Pharm. Prods., 868 A.2d 624, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 39 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Commonwealth of Pennsylvania brought an action on behalf of state agencies against defendant drug company. Plaintiff's claims under the Unfair Trade Practices and Consumer Protection Law (CPL), 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 201-1-201-9.3, were tried to the court, which ruled in favor of the Commonwealth. A jury returned a verdict in favor of defendant on the Commonwealth's common law claims. Both parties filed post-verdict motions.


OVERVIEW: The court stated that there was ample support for the finding that defendant violated 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 201-2(4)(xxi) by contributing to the reporting of inflated average wholesale prices for its drugs. Thus, it was bound to issue a permanent injunction under 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 201-4. The court held that an injunction could issue to restrain future conduct based on prior unlawful activity; that cessation of the offending conduct did not in and of itself bar a claim for injunctive relief; and that the court could consider whether the offending conduct was likely to reoccur absent the grant of an injunction. Nothing in § 201-4 prevented the Attorney General from suing on behalf of the agencies. Furthermore, the plain language of 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 201-4.1 did not preclude an award of restoration to the agencies. Defendant was not entitled to a stay of the injunction. Many of its arguments would become moot once the injunction was modified, and for it to continue to engage in unlawful conduct would harm the public. The Commonwealth was not entitled to costs or fees, as §§ 201-4 and 201-4.1 did not provide for such awards in an enforcement action by the Attorney General.


OUTCOME: The court denied the Commonwealth's post-trial motions. It denied defendant's post-trial motions except to modify the injunction to more closely conform it to that issued after a trial involving other defendants.

 

1745 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. TAP Pharm. Prods.


No. 212 M.D. 2004


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


36 A.3d 1112; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 414


May 9, 2011, Argued 
August 31, 2011, Decided 
August 31, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Vacated by, Remanded by Commonwealth v. Tap Pharm. Prods., 626 Pa. 25, 94 A.3d 364, 2014 Pa. LEXIS 1528 (2014)


PRIOR HISTORY: Commonwealth v. TAP Pharm. Prods., 868 A.2d 624, 2005 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 39 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff Commonwealth of Pennsylvania sued defendant pharmaceutical companies, alleging they caused two Commonwealth agencies to pay inflated prices for drugs. The Pennsylvania trial court found defendants violated Pennsylvania's Consumer Protection Law (CPL), 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 201-1 to 201-9.3. Plaintiff sought a new trial on its dismissed tort claims; defendants moved to vacate the awards of injunctive relief and restoration under the CPL.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff alleged defendants reported inflated average wholesale prices (AWPs) for certain drugs, thus damaging the agencies, which relied on these reported prices in reimbursing pharmacies for drugs provided to Medicaid recipients and certain elderly patients. The instant court held that 1) because the AWPs were inflated and fictitious, were not the prices intended by Pennsylvania's reimbursement laws, and had tendency to deceive those who dealt with the reimbursement system, defendants committed unfair or deceptive practices under 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 201-2(4)(xxi); 2) the practices were properly restrained under 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 201-4; and 3) restoration was properly ordered under 73 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 201-4.1. The trial court properly dismissed plaintiff's negligent misrepresentation claim on grounds the agencies did not rely on the accuracy of reported AWPs. It properly denied plaintiff's civil conspiracy claims since it did not find malice. Section 201-4.1 did not authorize an award of costs to plaintiff, and §§ 201-4, 201-4.1, which governed enforcement actions by the Pennsylvania Attorney General, did not authorize an award of attorney fees to plaintiff.


OUTCOME: Defendants' postjudgment motions were denied, as were plaintiff's motions for judgment notwithstanding the verdict or, alternately, for a new trial on the negligent misrepresentation and civil conspiracy claims, as well as its request to modify the award to include costs and attorney fees.

Leb. Valley Farmers Bank v. Commonwealth


No. 698 F.R. 2005


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


27 A.3d 288; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 380


September 16, 2009, Argued 
August 4, 2011, Decided 
August 4, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reversed by Leb. Valley Farmers Bank v. Commonwealth, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 3248 (Pa., Dec. 27, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 0504946. State Agency: Board of Finance and Revenue.
Leb. Valley Farmers Bank v. Commonwealth, 965 A.2d 1249, 2009 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 58 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner bank filed exceptions to the court's opinion and order affirming the denial of its petition for a refund by respondent, the Pennsylvania Board of Finance and Revenue, of a portion of the Shares Tax it paid for the 2002 tax year. The tax commonly referred to as the Shares Tax is set forth in Article VII of the Tax Reform Code, 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 7701 - 7706.


OVERVIEW: The bank raised a constitutional challenge to use of the combination provision under 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7701.1(c)(2) to calculate its Shares Tax, contending that because the provision applied only to institutions that had merged with another institution subject to the Shares Tax but not to institutions that had merged with out-of-state entities not subject to the tax, application of the combination provision violated both the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, and equal protection. The court concluded that a lack of uniformity was established as the lost value during the first few post merger years, and the tax which should have been paid on those values, is never recovered. However, the court did not agree with the bank's contention that the court sever the combination provision for all institutions. Instead, the court ordered that the averaging methodology required by 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7701.1(a) could be severed or limited when the taxable amount of shares results from the merger of an institution with a non-institution or an institution that has been in existence for fewer than six years without rendering the remainder of the Shares Tax incapable of execution.


OUTCOME: The court dismissed the bank's exceptions, ordered the prospective application of limited severance to the statutory provision identified, and ordered the Commonwealth to take necessary steps to provide meaningful retrospective relief in accordance with the court's opinion.

Sturgis v. Doe


No. 322 M.D. 2007


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


26 A.3d 1221; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 383


January 7, 2011, Submitted 
August 4, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY: Sturgis v. Doe, 600 Pa. 160, 963 A.2d 1291, 2009 Pa. LEXIS 290 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner inmate sought judgment on the pleadings with respect to his request for a writ of mandamus, requiring respondent, the Department of Corrections (DOC), to correct the starting date of his current prison sentence for third degree murder and aggravated assault. The matter was on remand from the Pennsylvania Supreme Court, which reversed a dismissal of the petition.


OVERVIEW: The inmate was previously convicted of aggravated assault and he was sentenced to a term of six years' imprisonment under the former Youthful Offenders Act (YOA), former 61 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 481-486 (repealed). Thereafter, the sentence was revised to a flat five-year sentence. However, the DOC calculated the dates of that sentence as a five-to-10-year sentence pursuant to the requirements of 42 Pa.C.S. § 9756(b). After serving the five-year term, the inmate was released on parole. Thereafter, he was convicted of two offenses and he was recommitted as a convicted parole violator. Accordingly, he was required to serve the backtime on his earlier conviction prior to beginning the new sentence of 15-to-30 years. The inmate sought mandamus relief, which was dismissed. On reversal and remand, the court found that the flat sentence was illegal because it contained a minimum sentence, in violation of the YOA. Further, the inmate had served the maximum sentence on the prior conviction, such that he was not on parole. The recommitment was accordingly error, and the court found that a change to the starting date was required to remedy the matter.


OUTCOME: The court granted the inmate's motion for judgment on the pleadings, and denied the DOC's request for summary relief.

 

1748 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Seitzinger v. Commonwealth


No. 838 M.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


25 A.3d 1299; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 345


January 14, 2011, Submitted 
July 28, 2011, Decided 
July 28, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Affirmed by Seitzinger v. Commonwealth, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 2255 (Pa., Sept. 28, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Court of ORIGINAL JURISDICTION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Pennsylvania Department of Labor and Industry, filed preliminary objections in the action brought by petitioners, a lawyer and his law firm, seeking declaratory and injunctive relief based on challenges to the attorney fee limitation provisions in the Pennsylvania Workers' Compensation Act, 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 998, and 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1000.5.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners asserted that the fee limitation provisions were unconstitutional under Pa. Const. art. V, § 10(c), the separation of powers doctrine, and violated due process. The court noted that 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 998 of the Act places a ceiling on the percentage of contingency fees attorneys may claim when successfully representing a claimant, and 77 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 1000.5 of the Act provides workers' compensation judges with the authority to approve and void compromise and release settlement agreements. The court held that neither provision was unconstitutional or violated the separation of powers doctrine. The court found that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court's explanatory comment that accompanied Pa. R. Prof. Conduct 1.5 specifically provided that the law may impose limitations on contingent fees. The court found that the Pennsylvania Supreme Court has made clear that laws that the Pennsylvania General Assembly adopts may set ceilings on the percentage of a workers' compensation settlement or award that an attorney may obtain in performing legal services for a claimant.


OUTCOME: The court sustained the Department's preliminary objections.

Gladstone Partners, LP v. E. Union Twp.

1750 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Guarrasi v. Scott


No. 630 M.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


25 A.3d 394; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 278


March 25, 2011, Submitted 
June 21, 2011, Decided 
June 21, 2011, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Related proceeding at Guarrasi v. Carroll, 620 Pa. 56, 66 A.3d 250, 2013 Pa. LEXIS 292 (2013)


PRIOR HISTORY: Commonwealth v. Guarrasi, 996 A.2d 542, 2010 Pa. Super. LEXIS 994 (Pa. Super. Ct., 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff inmate sued defendants judges and court officers for declarations (1) of his common law and constitutional rights of access to requested documents, (2) requiring those documents' release to him, and (3) that defendants violated his common law, statutory and constitutional rights by denying him access to the documents. Defendants filed preliminary objections.


OVERVIEW: The inmate's requests for court records were denied. The appellate court held he could not use a declaratory judgment suit to attack his criminal proceedings because, under 42 Pa.C.S. § 9542, the Post Conviction Relief Act (PCRA), 42 Pa.C.S. §§ 9541 - 9546, was his only remedy. He had no common law right to the records because the Right-To-Know Law (RTKL), 65 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 67.101 - 67.3104 and Pa.R.J.A. 509 controlled. The appellate court could not take jurisdiction because he did not exhaust his administrative remedies under 65 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 67.1101(a) or Pa.R.J.A. 509(c). His request to declare a judge was not another judge's designee for purposes of 18 Pa.C.S. § 5704(2)(iv) failed because this attacked his criminal convictions, which were only reviewable under the PCRA. His claim to recuse an entire trial court bench failed because (1) the appellate court lacked jurisdiction, under 42 Pa.C.S. § 761(b), and (2) his PCRA petition was the proper forum to address it. His claims to remove a judge as a trial court's RTKL appeals officer and to regulate who signed the trial court's orders failed because only the Pennsylvania Supreme Court could decide such claims.


OUTCOME: Defendants' preliminary objections were sustained, and the inmate's complaint was dismissed with prejudice.

In re Petition for Formation of Indep. Sch. Dist.


No. 2210 CD 2009


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


17 A.3d 977; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 167


September 14, 2010, Argued 
April 13, 2011, Decided 
April 13, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 2007-2132-31-6. Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks. Waite, J.
In re Petition for Formation of Indep. Sch. Dist., 962 A.2d 24, 2008 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 622 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner coalition appealed the order of the Common Pleas Court of the County of Bucks (Pennsylvania), which denied its petition to have their entire borough placed into the same school district. The trial court denied the petition because respondent, the Pennsylvania Secretary of Education, found that the coalition's proposal lacked merit from an educational standpoint.


OVERVIEW: The borough represented by the coalition was split between two school districts. They sought to have the entire borough placed into one, which was contiguous to the entire borough as opposed to the other district it was split into, which was not contiguous with any part of the borough. The Secretary had reasoned that in the interest of racial diversity, the eastern half of the borough should remain part of another district. The court found that the Secretary misapprehended and misapplied the applicable statutory law. The court noted that the Secretary's authority to pass on the merits of a petition was not open-ended but restricted under the Public School Code, 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 2-242.1(a), to the substantive provisions of the Public School Code. The court found that the coalition's petition, on its face, had educational merit because it would end the discontiguous boundaries of one district, which offended both the Public School Code, 24 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 2-201 and 2-242.1. Also, the affected children would be placed in a school district that produced better academic results and eliminated a long commute, if the coalition's evidence was credited on remand.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the order and remanded the case to the trial court with instructions to remand to the Secretary for further proceedings in accordance with the court's opinion.


 

1753 of 2746 DOCUMENTS



Evans v. Bureau of Prof'l and Occupational Affairs


No. 759 C.D. 2010


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


15 A.3d 99; 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 19


September 24, 2010, Submitted 
January 24, 2011, Decided 
January 24, 2011, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 58289-47-2009. State Agency: Bureau of Professional and Occupational Affairs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent, the Pennsylvania State Registration Board for Professional Engineers, Land Surveyors, and Geologists, ordered petitioner licensee to pay a civil penalty for doing the work of a professional geologist after his Pennsylvania license had lapsed. Petitioner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Pennsylvania Department of State, Bureau of Professional and Occupational Affairs, charged petitioner with violating, inter alia, § 4(e) (63 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 151(e)) of the Pennsylvania Engineer, Land Surveyor and Geologist Registration Law. Petitioner argued that the Board erred in imposing a civil penalty for this violation because his professional work was environmental, not geological and he did not hold himself out to the public as a licensed Pennsylvania geologist. The court held that the Bureau erred by charging petitioner with a "violation" of the § 4(e), the statutory definition of the "practice of geology"; it should have charged him with a violation of § 3(a) (63 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 150(a)) of the Law, which made it unlawful to practice geology without a license. Section 4(e) authorized the Board to enforce § 3(a), but § 4(e) was not a provision that was capable of being "violated" by a registered professional. To impose the penalties set forth in its regulation, 49 Pa. Code § 43b.13a, the Board had to charge petitioner with a violation of § 3(a). It did not do so. Without such a finding, the Board lacked any basis to invoke its civil penalty regulation.


OUTCOME: The order of the Board was reversed.

Boguslavsky v. N. Pocono Sch. Dist.

Both parties had filed for summary judgment in the matter.

Smith v. Carbon County Bd. of Assessment Appeals


No. 1205 C.D. 2009, No. 1326 C.D. 2009


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


10 A.3d 393; 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 663


December 7, 2009, Argued 
December 7, 2010, Decided 
December 7, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Appeal denied by Smith v. Carbon County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2011 Pa. LEXIS 1383 (Pa., June 21, 2011)
Appeal denied by Smith v. Carbon County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2011 Pa. LEXIS 1392 (Pa., June 21, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 07-3343. Common Pleas Court of the County of Carbon. Nanovic, President Judge.
Smith v. Carbon County Bd. of Assessment Appeals, 2009 Pa. Dist. & Cnty. Dec. LEXIS 95 (2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, a county board of assessment appeals (BAA) and a school district (SD), sought review of an order from the Common Pleas Court of the County of Carbon (Pennsylvania), which sustained a real estate tax assessment appeal of appellee condominium unit owner (CUO). The matter arose from the SD's challenge, and the BAA's resulting increase in assessment, to the unit's assessment for the 2008 tax year.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after the CUO's purchase, the SD filed an assessment appeal. The BAA sustained the appeal and increased the CUO's assessment based on application of the county's common level ratio to the current market value. On appeal to the trial court, the CUO presented his own testimony and that of a licensed real estate appraiser. The trial court sustained the appeal, concluding that pursuant to §§ 702 and 704 of the Assessment Law, 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. §§ 5453.702 and 5453.704, there were excessive assessments for properties conveyed after a particular date. The trial court found a violation of uniformity under Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1, and it reduced the assessment to that from the base year. On appeal, the appraiser's testimony regarding assessment ratios was deemed competent, and the CUO met his burden of proof. The evidence was thus sufficient to allow the trial court to determine the ratio of assessed value to market value of the comparable properties. However, the trial court erred in returning the assessment to the base year. Rather, the remedy for the lack of uniformity was a reduction in the assessment to conform to the common level prevailing in the tax district.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order of the trial court.

Borough of Walnutport v. Dennis


No. 1518 C.D. 2009


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


13 A.3d 541; 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 638


September 14, 2010, Argued 
December 3, 2010, Decided 
December 3, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Reargument denied by, En banc Borough of Walnutport v. Dennis, 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 18 (Pa. Commw. Ct., Jan. 21, 2011)
Appeal denied by Borough of Walnutport v. Dennis, 613 Pa. 654, 34 A.3d 84, 2012 Pa. LEXIS 4 (2012)
Appeal after remand at Borough of Walnutport v. Dennis, 63 A.3d 866, 2013 Pa. Commw. Unpub. LEXIS 247 (2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
Appealed from No. 1994-ML-8215. Common Pleas Court of County of Nothampton. Beltrami, J.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A property owner appealed from an order of the Court of Common Pleas of Northampton County (Pennsylvania) which entered an in rem judgment in the amount of $ 9,074.47 in favor of the Borough of Walnutport and against the property owner, stating that such judgment may be enforced pursuant to Pa.R.C.P. No. 3190, against certain property. Included in the judgment was attorney fees and judgment interest.


OVERVIEW: The property owner and his wife divorced. The wife conveyed her interest in the property to the property owner. However, the property owner never recorded the deed. A letter was sent to the property owner indicating that the property's street was scheduled for reconstruction and that curbing had to be installed. A signed certified receipt was returned to the Borough. The property owner did not install curbing. The Borough hired a contractor to install the curbing. The appellate court held the Borough met its burden of proof that it was entitled to collect attorney fees from the property owner. As the property owner lost his challenge, 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7187 mandated an award of reasonable attorney fees. The attorney fees of $ 1,183.62 as found by the trial court were reasonable and therefore, the Borough was entitled to such fees. The Borough's service was proper pursuant to 53 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7141. The property owner was the sole occupant of the premises. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in determining that the property owner had failed to prove that the property sustained damage as a result of the Borough's work in installing curbing abutting the property.


OUTCOME: The trial court was affirmed as to attorney fees, notice, property damage and the counterclaim. The trial court was reversed regarding the charging of interest.


 

1757 of 2746 DOCUMENTS


Delgaizo v. Commonwealth


No. 558 F.R. 2008, No. 37 F.R. 2009


COMMONWEALTH COURT OF PENNSYLVANIA


8 A.3d 429; 2010 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 615


October 14, 2010, Argued 
November 18, 2010, Decided 
November 18, 2010, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Adhered to, On reconsideration by, Judgment entered by DelGaizo v. Commonwealth, 23 A.3d 610, 2011 Pa. Commw. LEXIS 274 (Pa. Commw. Ct., 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY: Appealed from Nos. 0714248 and 0805736. State Agency Board of Finance and Revenue. Common Pleas Court.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner taxpayers filed applications for review against respondent Commonwealth of Pennsylvania after the Board of Finance and Revenue (Pennsylvania) upheld the Pennsylvania Department of Revenue's assessment of personal income tax against petitioners. Petitioners argued that 72 Pa. Stat. Ann. § 7307.10(b) violated the Equal Protection Clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and the Uniformity Clause, Pa. Const. art. VIII, § 1.


OVERVIEW: Petitioners held shares in a corporation, which the shareholders elected to treat as an S-corporation for taxation purposes. Petitioners argued that § 7307.10(b) unconstitutionally treated shareholders of Pennsylvania S-corporations differently than shareholders of Pennsylvania C-corporations by prohibiting carryover of losses by shareholders of Pennsylvania S-corporations while permitting carryover of losses by Pennsylvania C-corporations. The court stated that the legislature could not treat similarly situated taxpayers differently. S-corporations and C-corporations were not similarly situated taxpayers, however, by virtue of the S election and the financial benefits for which S election was made. Furthermore, a business entity could only become an S-corporation through election of its owners. The shareholders could not have the benefits of the S election without the burdens. Section 7307.10(b), therefore, did not violate the Uniformity Clause or the Equal Protection Clause.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the Board's decisions.
